The Five Universals
intermediate
7 min
Critique and Reconciliation of the Division of Universals
Commentator critiques the author's division of universals leading to seven, then reconciles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the apparent inconsistency in the author's division of universals and explain the commentator's reconciliation through conceptual unity.

Core Idea
The same universals can appear multiple times in a division; the number of concepts, not instances, determines the count.

The passage presents a critical refinement of the standard division of universals. The author's classification seems to yield seven universals, contradicting the accepted five. The commentator, Sharif, exposes the issue and then offers a reconciliation by distinguishing between conceptual categories and their instances. This lesson will guide you through the logical steps of this critique and resolution, sharpening your ability to analyse classifications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Trace the author's division
Start with the external universal. The author divides it into necessary (lāzim) and separable (mufāriq). Then each is divided into property (khāṣṣa) and general accident ('araḍ 'āmm). List the resulting subcategories.
Count them: necessary property, necessary accident, separable property, separable accident.
2
Identify the apparent inconsistency
The property under necessary is different from property under separable because they belong to different bearers. Thus there are four external subcategories, making seven universals when added to species, genus, and differentia. This contradicts the author's later statement that there are only five.
This is the commentator's initial objection.
3
Understand the commentator's reconciliation
The concept of property is the same whether it is necessary or separable: it is 'what is specific to one essence'. Similarly, general accident is 'what is not specific'. Therefore the four external subcategories reduce to just two concepts: property and accident. The total universals remain five.
This resolves the inconsistency by focusing on the essence (zubda) of the division.
4
Apply the lesson to other divisions
When dividing any set, distinguish between conceptual categories and their instantiations. A concept is not multiplied by being instantiated in different bearers. This principle is vital in classification sciences like fiqh and usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المصنف قسم الكلي الخارج عن الماهية إلى اللازم والمفارق، وقسم كلا منهما إلى الخاصة والعرض العام، فيكون الخارج عن الماهية منقسماً إلى أربعة أقسام، فيكون أقسام الكلي إذا سبعة على مقتضى تقسيمه - لا خمسة - فلا يصح قوله بعد ذلك: «فالكليات إذن خمس». (1) هذا في غاية الظهور، لأن المقسم يجب أن يكون معتبراً في كل واحد من أقسامه، فاللازم إذا قسم إلى خاصة وعرض عام فالقسمان هما: اللازم الذي هو خاصة واللازم الذي هو عرض عام؛ والمفارق إذا قسم إليهما كان القسمان: المفارق الذي هو خاصة والمفارق الذي هو عرض عام؛ فالخاصة والعرض العام اللذان وقعا قسمين للازم، غير الخاصة والعرض العام اللذين وقعا قسمين للمفارق. فأقسام الكلي الخارج أربعة على مقتضى تقسيمه، ومن أراد حصره في قسمين وجب عليه أن يقسمه أولا إلى الخاصة والعرض العام، ثم يقسم كل واحد منهما إلى اللازم والمفارق فيلزم انحصار الكلي في خمسة أقسام. وقد يعتذر للمصنف بأن اللازم انقسم إلى الخاصة والعرض العام باعتبار الاختصاص بماهية واحدة وعدم الاختصاص بها، والمفارق انقسم إليهما بهذا الاعتبار أيضاً، فعلم أن مفهوم الخاصة في اللازم والمفارق ما يختص بماهية واحدة، وأن مفهوم العرض العام فيهما ما لا يختص بها بل يعمها وغيرها. فقد رجع محصول الأقسام الأربعة إلى معنيين مطلقين يوجد كل منهما في اللازم والمفارق، وصار الكلي الخارج عن الماهية منحصراً فيهما، فإن لوحظ ظاهر التقسيم كان الأقسام أربعة، وإن لوحظ محصل تلك الأقسام رجعت إلى اثنين؛ فالشارح نظر إلى الظاهر فحكم بعدم صحة التفريع، والمصنف كأنه نظر إلى زبدة الأقسام في المآل، فلذلك فرع على تقسيمه الانحصار في الخمسة (شریف).
Know that the author divided the universal outside the essence into the necessary (lāzim) and the separable (mufāriq), and divided each of them into property (khāṣṣa) and general accident ('araḍ 'āmm). So the external becomes divided into four divisions, and thus the universals become seven according to his division – not five. So his statement after that is not valid: 'So the universals are five'. (1) This is extremely clear, because the divider must be considered in each of its divisions. So the necessary, when divided into property and general accident, the two divisions are: the necessary that is a property, and the necessary that is a general accident. And the separable, when divided into them, the two divisions are: the separable that is a property, and the separable that is a general accident. So the property and general accident that became divisions of the necessary are not the same as the property and general accident that became divisions of the separable. So the divisions of the external universal are four according to his division. And whoever wants to confine it to two divisions must first divide it into property and general accident, then divide each of them into the necessary and the separable, yielding the confinement of the universal to five divisions. And an excuse is made for the author: that the necessary divided into property and general accident by consideration of being specific to one essence or not, and the separable divided into them by this same consideration. So it is known that the concept of property in the necessary and the separable is what is specific to one essence, and the concept of general accident in both is what is not specific to it but includes it and others. So the upshot of the four divisions returns to two absolute meanings, each found in the necessary and separable. And the external universal becomes confined to them. So if the outward division is considered, the divisions are four; and if the summary of those divisions is considered, they reduce to two. So the commentator looked at the outward and judged the inference invalid, while the author apparently looked at the essence of the divisions in the final outcome, and therefore based on his division he inferred the confinement to five (Sharīf).
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing an apparent inconsistency in the author's division of universals. The author had previously defined five universals: species, genus, differentia, property, and general accident. However, in his classification, he divided the external universal (that which is outside the essence) into necessary (lāzim) and separable (mufāriq), and then subdivided each into property and general accident. This yields four subcategories for the external universal alone, making the total universals seven (species, genus, differentia, plus four from external). The commentator points out this apparent error, arguing that the property and general accident under 'necessary' are different from those under 'separable', so they count as distinct universals. Then the commentator offers a reconciliation: if we look at the conceptual essence (زبدة), property and general accident are the same concepts whether they attach to necessary or separable. The property is always what is specific to one essence, and the general accident is always what is not specific. Thus, the four external divisions reduce to just two concepts: property and general accident. Therefore, the total universals remain five: species, genus, differentia, property, general accident. The commentator (Sharīf) notes that the apparent contradiction arises from looking at the surface division versus the deeper conceptual unity. This is a classic example of how logical texts handle potential inconsistencies through nuanced analysis of the division's intention.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator initially argue that the author's division leads to seven universals?
A) Because the author divided the external universal into four subcategories.
B) Because the author added two more universals to the five.
C) Because the author included species and genus twice.
D) Because the author forgot that property and accident are the same.

Key Takeaway
The apparent proliferation of universals from division is resolved by recognizing that property and accident are conceptual categories, not multiplied by their attachment to different bearers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the method shown in this passage, analyse the following division: 'Propositions are divided into categorical and conditional; each is divided into affirmative and negative.' How many types of propositions result from a surface reading? How many concepts are actually present? Write a brief explanation.
?
In a discussion of the five universals, a student claims that since 'general accident' can be either necessary or separable, there are actually six universals. Using the commentator's reasoning, explain why this claim is mistaken and show that the universals remain five.

The Five Universals

Reconciling Apparent Divisions in the Five Universals
Commentator defends the five universals by reconciling apparent divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve apparent contradictions in classification systems by distinguishing between formal divisions and conceptual meanings.

Core Idea
Four divisions can reduce to two when meaning is considered.

In this passage, the commentator presents a problem: if external universals are divided into necessary and separable, and each into property and common accident, we get four divisions—conflicting with the claim of five universals. A marginal note by al-Sharif shows how this conflict is resolved by looking at the conceptual meaning behind the formal division. You will learn to apply this logical move in your own analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formal division
The passage divides the external universal (al-kulli al-kharij) into lazim and mufariq, then each into khassa and 'arad 'amm. Notice that this yields four categories: lazim-khassa, lazim-'arad 'amm, mufariq-khassa, mufariq-'arad 'amm.
2
Spot the apparent contradiction
Adding these four to the three essential universals (genus, species, differentia) gives seven, which contradicts the author's statement that the universals are five.
3
Distinguish form from meaning
Al-Sharif argues that the concepts of khassa and 'arad 'amm are the same whether they apply to lazim or mufariq. 'Khassa' always means 'specific to one essence' and 'arad 'amm' means 'common to many essences'. Thus the four divisions reduce to two concepts: khassa and 'arad 'amm.
This is the key step: recognize that the same meaning appears in multiple branches.
4
Apply the reconciliation
By considering the muhassal (product) of the divisions rather than the zahir (apparent form), the external universals reduce to two: khassa and 'arad 'amm. Together with the three essential universals, the total remains five.
This reconciles the author's claim with the division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمفارق، وقسم كلا منهما إلى الخاصة والعرض العام، فيكون الخارج عن الماهية منقسماً إلى أربعة أقسام، فيكون أقسام الكلي إذا سبعة على مقتضى تقسيمه - لا خمسة) -
فلا يصح قوله بعد ذلك : «فالكليات إذن خمس».
(١) هذا في غاية الظهور ، لأن المقسم يجب أن يكون معتبراً في كل واحد من أقسامه، فاللازم إذا قسم إلى خاصة وعرض عام فالقسمان هما : اللازم الذي هو خاصة واللازم الذي هو عرض عام ؛ والمفارق إذا قسم إليهما كان القسمان : المفارق الذي هو خاصة والمفارق الذي هو عرض عام ؛ فالخاصة والعرض العام اللذان وقعا قسمين للازم، غير الخاصة والعرض العام اللذين وقعا قسمين للمفارق.
فأقسام الكلّيّ الخارج أربعة على مقتضى تقسيمه، ومن أراد حصره في قسمين وجب عليه أن يقسمه أولا إلى الخاصة والعرض العام، ثم يقسم كل واحد منهما إلى اللازم والمفارق فيلزم انحصار الكلي في خمسة أقسام. وقد يعتذر للمصنف بأن اللازم انقسم إلى الخاصة والعرض العام باعتبار الاختصاص بماهية واحدة وعدم الاختصاص بها، والمفارق انقسم إليهما بهذا الاعتبار أيضاً، فعلم أن مفهوم الخاصة في اللازم والمفارق ما يختص بماهية واحدة، وأن مفهوم العرض العام فيهما ما لا يختص بها بل يعمها وغيرها. فقد رجع محصول الأقسام الأربعة إلى معنيين مطلقين يوجد كل منهما في اللازم والمفارق، وصار الكلّي الخارج عن الماهية منحصراً فيهما، فإن لوحظ ظاهر التقسيم كان الأقسام أربعة ، وإن لوحظ محصل تلك الأقسام رجعت إلى اثنين ؛ فالشارح نظر إلى الظاهر فحكم بعدم صحة التفريع، والمصنف كأنه نظر إلى زيدة الأقسام في المآل، فلذلك فرع على تقسيمه الانحصار في الخمسة (شریف).
And the separable, and he divided each of them into property and common accident, so what is outside the essence becomes divided into four divisions, so the divisions of the universal become seven according to his division - not five. So his statement thereafter 'Thus the universals are five' is not valid. (1) This is extremely clear, because the divided must be considered in each of its divisions. So when the necessary is divided into property and common accident, the two divisions are: the necessary which is property and the necessary which is common accident. And when the separable is divided into them, the two divisions are: the separable which is property and the separable which is common accident. So the property and common accident that occur as divisions of the necessary are different from the property and common accident that occur as divisions of the separable. Thus the divisions of the universal outside are four according to his division. And whoever wants to confine it to two divisions must first divide it into property and common accident, then divide each of them into necessary and separable, so the universal is confined to five divisions. And one may apologize for the author by saying that the necessary was divided into property and common accident in consideration of being specific to one essence and not being specific to it, and the separable was divided into them by this same consideration. So it is known that the concept of property in the necessary and the separable is what is specific to one essence, and the concept of common accident in them is what is not specific to it but rather general to it and others. So the product of the four divisions returns to two absolute meanings, each of which exists in the necessary and the separable, and the universal outside the essence becomes confined to them. So if the apparent division is considered, the divisions are four; and if the product of those divisions is considered, they return to two. So the commentator (sharih) looked at the apparent and judged that the inference is not valid, and the author (musannif) apparently looked at the core of the divisions in the final analysis, therefore he inferred from his division the confinement to five. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the apparent contradiction between the division of universals and the claim that there are only five. The commentator explains that if you divide the external universal (al-kulli al-kharij) first into necessary (lazim) and separable (mufariq), then each into property (khassa) and common accident ('arad 'amm), you get four divisions, which would make the total seven when added to the essential universals (genus, species, differentia). This contradicts the author's statement that the universals are five. The marginal note (by al-Sharif) then defends the author by distinguishing between the apparent division and the essential meaning. The two divisions of property and common accident under necessary and separable are actually two absolute concepts: property means being specific to one essence, and common accident means being general to many. So the four divisions reduce to two. If you consider the outer form of the division, you get four; if you consider the underlying meaning, they reduce to two, thus preserving the five universals. This is a careful logical exercise showing how a formal division can appear to multiply terms while the conceptual content remains the same.

Key Takeaway
The key logical move is to distinguish between the apparent multiplication of categories in a formal division and the actual conceptual content, which may reduce to fewer core meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a fiqh manual, you find that rulings are classified into wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, haram. Then each is subdivided into definite and probable. Count the total categories and then reduce them to the core meanings. Write a short explanation of how the subdivisions return to the original five.
?
Consider the classification of causes in usul al-fiqh into 'illa, sabab, sharṭ, māni'. Each can be further divided into certain and probable. Show that the total number of distinct causal concepts remains four by distinguishing the formal division from the conceptual meaning.

Impossible and Possible Non-Existent Universals
Author explains first two existential categories: impossible and possible non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a universal that is impossible in external reality (mumtani' al-wujud) and one that is possible but non-existent (mumkin al-wujud without actual existence), using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Impossible universals are not conceptually contradictory, but real-world impossibilities like a partner to Allah.

This lesson focuses on the first two of the five existential categories of universals: the impossible and the possible but non-existent. The author gives clear examples: a partner to Allah (sharik al-Bari) is impossible externally, while the mythical 'anqa bird is possible but does not exist. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct metaphysical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first category
Read the first category: 'The universal may be impossible of existence in the external world not because of the very concept of the word, like the associate of Allah.' Notice that the impossibility is not due to a contradiction in the concept itself, but due to external reality.
In kalam, such impossibility is often classified as 'mumtani' li-ghayrihi' (impossible due to another).
2
Identify the second category
Read the second category: 'And it may be possible of existence but does not exist, like the 'anqa.' This refers to something that is not impossible in itself, but happens not to exist in reality.
The 'anqa is a standard example in Islamic logic for a non-existent possible universal.
3
Compare the two categories
Ask yourself: What is the key difference? The first is impossible because of external factors (e.g., sharik al-Bari is impossible because Allah cannot have a partner), while the second is possible in itself but simply does not occur.
Both are universals that do not exist externally, but the reason differs.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Consider the universal 'prophet after Muhammad (s)' or 'angel equal to Jibril.' Are these impossible or possible but non-existent? Apply the same reasoning.
Use the concept of continuity of prophethood (khatm al-nubuwwa) for the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢٦] قال :
الفصل الثالث
في مباحث الكلّي والجزئي)
ممتنع الوجود وممكن الوجود وأقسامه]
وهي
خمسة:
الأول: الكلّي قد يكون ممتنع الوجود في الخارج لا لنفس مفهوم اللفظ كشريك الباري عز اسمه.
وقد يكون ممكن الوجود لكن لا يوجد، كالعنقاء.
وقد يكون الموجود منه واحداً فقط مع امتناع غيره – كالباري عز اسمه أو مع إمكانه – كالشمس.
وقد يكون الموجود منه كثيراً: إما متناهياً كالكواكب السبعة السيارة، أو غير متناه كالنفوس الناطقة عند بعضهم.
(1) ذكر الجزئي هاهنا على سبيل التبعية، إذ قد سبق أن ليس لصاحب هذا الفن غرض متعلّق بالجزئيات، فلا بحث له عن أحوال الجزئي، لكنه تصور مفهوميه - أعني الحقيقي الذي مضى والإضافي الذي سنذكره - وبين النسبة بين مفهوميه تتميماً للتصوير، وما يبين النسبة بين الإضافي والكلّي أيضاً توضيحاً لتصويره (شریف).
[26] He said: The third chapter on the topics of the universal and particular. [Impossible existence and possible existence and its divisions] And they are five: First: The universal may be impossible of existence in the external world not because of the very concept of the word, like the associate of Allah, may His name be exalted. And it may be possible of existence but does not exist, like the 'anqa. And there may be only one existent of it, either with the impossibility of another – like Allah, may His name be exalted – or with its possibility – like the sun. And there may be many existents of it: either limited like the seven wandering planets, or unlimited like the rational souls according to some. (1) He mentioned the particular here by way of subordination, for it has already preceded that the practitioner of this art has no purpose related to particulars, so there is no investigation of the states of the particular. But he conceived its two concepts – I mean the real which has passed and the relative which we will mention – and explained the relation between its two concepts to complete the conceptualization, and what clarifies the relation between the relative and the universal is also an elucidation of its conceptualization. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the five existential categories of universals. The chapter discusses the universal and the particular, but the focus is on the universal. The first category is a universal that is impossible in external existence, such as a partner to Allah (sharik al-Bari) – not because the word itself is contradictory, but because the reality is impossible. The second is a universal that is possible in essence but does not happen to exist, like the mythical 'anqa bird. The third is a universal that exists in only one instance: either necessarily unique like Allah, or possibly unique like the sun. The fourth is a universal that has many instances: either finite, like the seven planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, etc.), or infinite, like rational souls according to some philosophers. The marginal note explains that the particular is mentioned only incidentally, since logic primarily deals with universals, but the author clarifies the two types of particular (real and relative) for completeness. This classification is essential for understanding how universals relate to existence in the external world.

Key Terms
ممتنع الوجود
ممكن الوجود
عنقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'a partner to Allah' impossible?
A) Because the concept 'partner of Allah' is self-contradictory
B) Because it does not exist in external reality, but is possible in itself
C) Because Allah cannot have a partner; it is externally impossible
D) Because it is a particular, not a universal

Key Takeaway
The first two existential categories of universals are: impossible existence (due to external reality) and possible but non-existent existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Universals with a Single Instance: Necessity and Contingency
Author explains the third existential category: a universal with only one instance, either necessarily or contingently unique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify universals that have only one instance and explain whether that uniqueness is necessary (like Allah) or contingent (like the sun).

Core Idea
A universal can have exactly one instance, either necessarily (Allah) or contingently (the sun).

The third existential category covers universals that exist in only one instance. The author distinguishes two subcategories: when uniqueness is necessary because another instance is impossible, like Allah, and when uniqueness is contingent because another instance is possible, like the sun. This classification is vital for understanding how universals relate to their instances.

Guided Path
1
Locate the third category in the text
Find the phrase: 'there may be only one existent of it, either with the impossibility of another – like Allah, or with its possibility – like the sun.' This is the third of the five categories.
2
Understand the two subtypes
Analyze the two cases: (a) Necessary uniqueness: the universal has exactly one instance, and no other instance can exist, e.g., Allah. (b) Contingent uniqueness: the universal has exactly one instance, but other instances are possible, e.g., the sun (since Allah could create another sun).
In kalam, Allah's uniqueness is tied to His essence (wajib al-wujud); the sun's uniqueness is accidental.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider the universal 'the last Prophet of Islam (Muhammad s)'. Is his uniqueness necessary or contingent? Use kalam principles to decide.
According to Islamic belief, prophethood ended with Muhammad (s), so uniqueness here is necessary in terms of prophethood.
4
Contrast with the first two categories
Note that this category is about a universal that does exist, unlike the first two categories which are non-existent. The focus shifts from existence to number of instances.

Source Text

[٢٦] قال :
الفصل الثالث
في مباحث الكلّي والجزئي)
ممتنع الوجود وممكن الوجود وأقسامه]
وهي
خمسة:
الأول: الكلّي قد يكون ممتنع الوجود في الخارج لا لنفس مفهوم اللفظ كشريك الباري عز اسمه.
وقد يكون ممكن الوجود لكن لا يوجد، كالعنقاء.
وقد يكون الموجود منه واحداً فقط مع امتناع غيره – كالباري عز اسمه أو مع إمكانه – كالشمس.
وقد يكون الموجود منه كثيراً: إما متناهياً كالكواكب السبعة السيارة، أو غير متناه كالنفوس الناطقة عند بعضهم.
(1) ذكر الجزئي هاهنا على سبيل التبعية، إذ قد سبق أن ليس لصاحب هذا الفن غرض متعلّق بالجزئيات، فلا بحث له عن أحوال الجزئي، لكنه تصور مفهوميه - أعني الحقيقي الذي مضى والإضافي الذي سنذكره - وبين النسبة بين مفهوميه تتميماً للتصوير، وما يبين النسبة بين الإضافي والكلّي أيضاً توضيحاً لتصويره (شریف).
[26] He said: The third chapter on the topics of the universal and particular. [Impossible existence and possible existence and its divisions] And they are five: First: The universal may be impossible of existence in the external world not because of the very concept of the word, like the associate of Allah, may His name be exalted. And it may be possible of existence but does not exist, like the 'anqa. And there may be only one existent of it, either with the impossibility of another – like Allah, may His name be exalted – or with its possibility – like the sun. And there may be many existents of it: either limited like the seven wandering planets, or unlimited like the rational souls according to some. (1) He mentioned the particular here by way of subordination, for it has already preceded that the practitioner of this art has no purpose related to particulars, so there is no investigation of the states of the particular. But he conceived its two concepts – I mean the real which has passed and the relative which we will mention – and explained the relation between its two concepts to complete the conceptualization, and what clarifies the relation between the relative and the universal is also an elucidation of its conceptualization. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the five existential categories of universals. The chapter discusses the universal and the particular, but the focus is on the universal. The first category is a universal that is impossible in external existence, such as a partner to Allah (sharik al-Bari) – not because the word itself is contradictory, but because the reality is impossible. The second is a universal that is possible in essence but does not happen to exist, like the mythical 'anqa bird. The third is a universal that exists in only one instance: either necessarily unique like Allah, or possibly unique like the sun. The fourth is a universal that has many instances: either finite, like the seven planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, etc.), or infinite, like rational souls according to some philosophers. The marginal note explains that the particular is mentioned only incidentally, since logic primarily deals with universals, but the author clarifies the two types of particular (real and relative) for completeness. This classification is essential for understanding how universals relate to existence in the external world.

Key Terms
ممتنع الوجود
ممكن الوجود
جزئي حقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Allah is unique because He is impossible, while the sun is possible
B) Allah's uniqueness is necessary because another instance is impossible, while the sun's uniqueness is contingent because another instance is possible
C) Both are necessary because only one exists
D) The sun is not a universal

Key Takeaway
The third category of universals covers those with exactly one instance, which may be necessarily unique (like Allah) or contingently unique (like the sun).

Universals with Many Instances: Finite and Infinite
Author explains the fourth existential category: a universal with many instances, finite or infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universals that have finite instances (like the seven planets) and those that have infinite instances (like rational souls), and recognize that infinity of instances is a debated topic.

Core Idea
Universals can have many instances, either finite (e.g., planets) or infinite (e.g., souls), but the latter is only accepted by some.

The fourth and final existential category of universals discussed here is those that exist in many instances. The author further divides this into finite many (like the seven planets) and potentially infinite many (like rational souls, according to some). This touches on debates in Islamic philosophy about the possibility of actual infinity and the nature of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Locate the fourth category in the text
Find: 'there may be many existents of it: either limited like the seven wandering planets, or unlimited like the rational souls according to some.' This is the fifth category (though the author lists it as 'and there may be many'). Actually, the enumeration is: first, second, third (single instance), fourth (many). But the text says 'fifth'? It says 'وهي خمسة' and then lists four? Let's count: first (impossible), second (possible non-existent), third (one instance), fourth (many). That's four. Possibly the 'وقد يكون الموجود منه واحداً' is the third and 'وقد يكون الموجود منه كثيرا' is the fourth. But the text says 'وهي خمسة'? Actually it says 'وهي خمسة' and then in the translation 'And they are five' but only four are numbered? The Arabic: 'وهي خمسة:' then 'الأول: الكلّي قد يكون ممتنع الوجود...' then 'وقد يكون ممكن الوجود...' then 'وقد يكون الموجود منه واحداً...' then 'وقد يكون الموجود منه كثيراً'. That's four. Perhaps the fifth is the marginal note? Or the author considers the divisions differently? The commentary says 'five existential categories' but only lists four. There is a missing fifth? Possibly the fifth is 'the particular'? But the marginal note says the particular is mentioned incidentally. This is a possible confusion. However, the source_chunk clearly says 'five' but enumerates four lines. The fifth might be the note itself? Actually, in the original text, after the fourth, the note is inserted. The five categories could be: 1. ممتنع الوجود, 2. ممكن الوجود لكن لا يوجد, 3. الموجود منه واحد (مع امتناع غيره), 4. الموجود منه واحد (مع إمكان غيره), 5. الموجود منه كثير (منقسم إلى متناهٍ وغير متناه). That would make five: the third category is split into two subcategories. So the five are: (1) impossible, (2) possible non-existent, (3) one instance with impossibility, (4) one instance with possibility, (5) many instances (with subcategories finite/infinite). This is confirmed by the structure: the author lists 'وقد يكون الموجود منه واحداً فقط مع امتناع غيره – كالباري عز اسمه أو مع إمكانه – كالشمس.' The 'أو' indicates two subcases. So the five are: 1,2,3a,3b,4 (where 4 is many). So our lesson on the third category actually covers 3a and 3b. This lesson on many instances covers the fifth category. To avoid confusion, we can refer to it as the fourth category in the enumeration (since most scholars treat it as the fourth type after the singular ones). The text says 'وقد يكون الموجود منه كثيراً' so it is the last of the series. I'll call it 'the fourth category' in the lesson title, but clarify in the commentary.
2
Understand the two subcategories of many instances
The author divides this into: (a) finite many, like the seven planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, etc.), and (b) unlimited (infinite) many, like rational souls according to some philosophers.
The phrase 'according to some' indicates a point of disagreement; in Islamic theology, many denied actual infinity.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the universal 'planet' in the context of the seven planets (classical astronomy). Why is it finite? Also, why would rational souls be infinite? What are the theological implications?
The seven planets refers to the classical known planets. Unknowingly, there are many more, but the example still illustrates finiteness in principle.
4
Position within the five categories
Understand how this category fits with the others: it deals with universals that have multiple actual instances, as opposed to unique or non-existent ones.

Classifying Universals by Their Individuals
Author classifies universals by number and finitude of their external individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal according to the number and finitude of its external individuals.

Core Idea
A universal can have one individual or many, and if many, they can be finite or infinite.

The author explains that universals are not all alike in their relationship to individuals. Some universals have only one individual in the external world, while others have many. Among those with one, it may be impossible for another to exist, like the Creator, or possible, like the sun. Among those with many, their individuals may be finite, like the moving planets, or infinite, like the rational soul according to some philosophers. This classification helps us understand the scope of universal concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal
Find the term being discussed. In the passage, examples are al-Bari', al-shams, al-kawkab al-sayyar, and al-nafs al-natiqa.
2
Determine number of individuals
Check if the universal has one individual or many in the external world. The passage distinguishes these two cases first.
One individual: either impossible or possible for another. Many: either finite or infinite.
3
Apply the classification to a new example
Take a universal like 'nabi' (prophet). It has many individuals but finite. Categorize it under 'many, finite'.
Use Islamic examples from 'aqida or fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الكلى والجزئي الأفراد فيه، فإن لم يكن متعدّد الأفراد في الخارج بل يكون منحصراً في فرد واحد : فلا يخلو إما أن يكون مع امتناع غيره من الأفراد في الخارج أو يكون مع إمكان غيره ؛ الأول كالباري عن اسمه، والثاني كالشمس. وإن كان له أفراد متعدّدة موجودة في الخارج، فإما أن يكون أفراده متناهية أو غير متناهية، والأول كالكوكب السيار فإنه كلّي له أفراد منحصر في الكواكب السبعة السيّارة، والثاني كالنفس الناطقة فإن أفرادها غير متناهية على مذهب بعض الفلاسفة.
Discussions on the universal and particular. Its individuals: if it does not have multiple individuals in the external world but is confined to a single individual, then it is either with the impossibility of another individual in the external world or with the possibility of another. The first is like the Necessary Being (glory to His name), and the second is like the sun. And if it has multiple individuals existing in the external world, then either its individuals are finite or infinite. The first is like the moving planet, for it is a universal having individuals confined to the seven moving planets. The second is like the rational soul, for its individuals are infinite according to some philosophers.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage classifies universals based on the number and finitude of their individuals in the external world. The author first considers a universal that has only one individual. This can be of two types: one where it is impossible for any other individual to exist, such as the Necessary Being (Allah), and one where another individual is possible but not actual, such as the sun (which is unique in our solar system but could theoretically have another sun). Next, if a universal has multiple individuals, those individuals are either finite in number, like the universal 'moving planet' which has only the seven known planets, or infinite, like the rational soul according to philosophers who believe souls are infinite. This classification is important for understanding the nature of universals.

The Three Universals: Natural, Logical, Mental
Author defines three types of universals based on considering essence, universality, and their composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of universals (natural, logical, mental) and identify which one exists in the external world according to the author.

Core Idea
Universals can be viewed as pure essence, as the property of universality, or as their combination.

When we say 'animal is a universal', we are actually referring to three distinct aspects. The logician distinguishes between the essence itself, the concept of being universal, and the composite. This distinction is foundational for understanding how universals exist and are used in definitions across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects
When we say 'animal is a universal', the passage lists three things: animal insofar as it is itself (essence), its being universal (universality), and the composite of the two.
2
Name each universal
Match each aspect to its technical name: the essence is called the natural universal, the universality is called the logical universal, and the composite is called the mental universal.
Memorize the Arabic terms: كلي طبيعي, كلي منطقي, كلي عقلي.
3
Determine external existence
The natural universal exists in the external world because it is part of the concrete individual (e.g., 'animal' as part of this horse). The other two are concepts in the mind.
The author explicitly says the natural universal exists externally; the others are disputed but outside logic's scope.
4
Recognize the scope of logic
The existence of the logical and mental universals is not discussed in logic; it belongs to metaphysics. Logic only deals with the concepts as tools for reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلي الطبيعي والمنطقي والعقلي قال : الثاني: إذا قلنا للحيوان مثلاً بأنه كلّي، فهناك أمور ثلاثة: الحيوان من حيث هوهو، وكونه كلّيّاً، والمركب منهما؛ والأوّل يسمّى «كليّاً طبيعياً»، والثاني يسمّى «كلّيّاً منطقياً»، والثالث يسمّى «كلياً عقلياً». والكلّي الطبيعي موجود في الخارج لأنه جزء من هذا الحيوان الموجود في الخارج، وجزء الموجود موجود في الخارج وأما الكليان الآخران ففي وجودهما في الخارج خلاف، والنظر فيه خارج عن المنطق.
The natural, logical, and mental universal. He said: Second: When we say, for example, 'animal is a universal', there are three things: animal insofar as it is itself, its being universal, and the composite of the two. The first is called the natural universal, the second is called the logical universal, and the third is called the mental universal. The natural universal exists in the external world because it is a part of this animal existing in the external world, and a part of what exists exists in the external world. As for the other two universals, there is disagreement concerning their existence in the external world, and consideration of that is outside logic.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage introduces the famous distinction between three types of universals from the perspective of mental analysis. When we predicate 'universal' of 'animal', we can consider the essence of animal itself (animal as animal), the attribute of universality (being universal), and the combination of the two (animal as universal). The first, the essence considered in itself, is called the natural universal (kulli tabi'i). The second, the concept of universality, is called the logical universal (kulli mantiqi). The third, the composite, is called the mental universal (kulli 'aqli). The author then states that the natural universal exists in the external world because it is part of the concrete animal; the other two have disputed ontological status, but this question is beyond the scope of logic.

Key Terms
كلي طبيعي
كلي منطقي
كلي عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Logical universal
B) Natural universal
C) Mental universal
D) All three

Key Takeaway
Only the natural universal (essence considered in itself) exists in the external world; the logical and mental universals are concepts in the mind.

Distinguishing the Three Universals: The Analogy of Whiteness and White
Commentator expands on the three universals using the analogy of whiteness and white.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the natural, logical, and mental universals using the analogy of whiteness and white, and apply this distinction to any concept in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The universal is an accident added to a nature, just as white is an accident added to a garment.

In this commentary, the commentator clarifies the three entities involved when we say 'animal is universal': the animal-nature itself, the concept of universality, and their composite. He uses a striking analogy from everyday perception: whiteness and white. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise definition in Islamic logic, as it prevents conflating a thing's essence with its universal property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three entities
The passage lists three things: animal in itself (the nature), the concept of universal (without reference to any particular matter), and the composite of animal and universal. Recognize each as the natural, logical, and mental universal respectively.
The natural universal is the essence considered in itself; the logical universal is the property of being universal; the mental universal is the combination.
2
Understand the definition of 'universal'
The commentator defines 'universal' as 'that whose very conception does not prevent sharing in it'. This is the standard logical definition: a concept that can apply to multiple individuals.
This definition is key to understanding why universality is an accidental attribute of a concept.
3
Analyze the analogy with whiteness and white
The analogy compares the relation between universality and animal to the relation between whiteness and a garment. From whiteness we derive 'white' which is predicated of the garment. Similarly, from universality we derive 'universal' which is predicated of animal. Just as 'white' is not identical to 'garment', 'universal' is not identical to 'animal'.
The analogy shows that the logical universal is an accidental attribute added to the natural universal.
4
Apply the analogy to another concept
Take a concept like 'human'. What are the three universals? Human in itself (the nature), the universal property (concept of universal), and the composite 'human universal'. Use the analogy to explain why 'universal' is not part of the definition of human.
This reinforces that universality is an accidental concept, not part of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : إذا قلنا : «الحيوان - مثلاً - كلّي» فهناك أمور ثلاثة : الحيوان من حيث هو هو، ومفهوم الكلّي من غير إشارة إلى مادة من المواد، والحيوان الكلّي وهو المجموع المركب منهما - أي من الحيوان والكلي. والتغاير بين هذه المفهومات ظاهر، فإنه لو كان المفهوم من أحدهما عين المفهوم من الآخر لزم من تعقل أحدهما تعقل الآخر؛ وليس كذلك، فإن مفهوم الكلّي «مالا يمنع نفس تصوره عن وقوع الشركة فيه»، والحاصل أن مفهوم الحيوان - أعني الجوهر القابل للأبعاد النامي الحساس المتحرك بالإرادة - أمر يعرضه في العقل حالة اعتبارية هي كونه غير مانع من الشركة، فنسبة هذا العارض المسمّى بالكلية إلى ذلك المعروض في العقل كنسبة البياض العارض للثوب في الخارج إليه، فإذا اشتق من البياض «الأبيض» المحمول بالمواطأة على الثوب كان هناك معروض هو «الثوب» وعارض هو مفهوم «الأبيض» ومجموع المركب من المعروض والعارض ؛ كذلك إذا اشتق من الكلية الكلّيّ المحمول بالمواطأة على الحيوان، كان هناك أيضاً معروض هو مفهوم الحيوان» وعارض هو مفهوم الكلي» ومجموع المركب من المعروض والعارض. وكما أن مفهوم «الأبيض» من حيث هو ليس عين مفهوم «الشوب» ولا جزء له - بل هو مفهوم وخارج عنه صالح لأن يحمل على الثوب وعلى غيره - كذلك مفهوم الكلي» ليس عين مفهوم «الحيوان» ولاجزء له، بل مفهوم خارج عنه صالح لأن يحمل على الحيوان وعلى غيره من المفهومات التي تعرضها الكلية في العقل (شريف).
The statement: When we say 'animal, for example, is universal', there are three things: animal insofar as it is itself, the concept of universal without reference to any matter, and the universal animal which is the composite of the two, i.e., of animal and universal. The distinction among these concepts is apparent, for if the concept of one were identical to the concept of the other, then conceiving one would necessitate conceiving the other; but that is not the case, for the concept of universal is 'that whose very conception does not prevent sharing in it'. The conclusion is that the concept of animal—meaning the substance capable of dimensions, growing, sentient, moving by will—is something that is accidentally affected in the mind by an accidental state, namely its being not preventing sharing. So the relation of this accident called 'universality' to that subject in the mind is like the relation of whiteness accidentally affecting the garment in the external world to it. When from whiteness is derived 'white' which is predicated equivalently of the garment, there is a subject which is 'garment', an accident which is the concept 'white', and a composite of subject and accident. Similarly, when from universality is derived 'universal' predicated equivalently of animal, there is also a subject which is the concept of animal, an accident which is the concept of universal, and a composite of subject and accident. And just as the concept 'white' in itself is not identical to the concept 'garment' nor a part of it—rather it is a concept external to it, valid for predication of the garment and other things—so too the concept 'universal' is not identical to the concept 'animal' nor a part of it; rather it is a concept external to it, valid for predication of animal and other concepts that are accidentally affected by universality in the mind. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the passage defining the three universals. The commentator begins by restating the three entities: animal in itself (the natural universal), the concept of universal (the logical universal), and the composite (the mental universal). He then argues that these concepts are distinct because if they were identical, knowledge of one would imply knowledge of the other, but that is not the case. He explains that 'universal' is defined as 'that whose very conception does not prevent sharing' (the standard definition). Then he uses an elegant analogy: the relationship between universality and the concept of animal in the mind is like the relationship between whiteness and a garment in the external world. From whiteness we derive the term 'white' which is predicated of the garment; from universality we derive 'universal' which is predicated of animal. Just as 'white' is not identical to 'garment', so 'universal' is not identical to 'animal'. This clarifies that the logical universal (universality) is an accidental concept added to the natural universal (animal).

Key Terms
الكلّي (al-kulli)
الطبيعي (al-tabi'i)
العقلي (al-'aqli)
المحمول بالمواطأة (al-mahmul bil-muwata'a)
العارض (al-'arad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) One is a part of the other
C) They are distinct and the universal is an accidental concept predicated of animal
D) They are mutually exclusive

Key Takeaway
The three universals are conceptually distinct: the natural universal is the essence, the logical universal is the accidental property of being shareable, and the mental universal is their composite; the analogy with whiteness and white solidifies this distinction.

The Three Concepts of the Universal
Author distinguishes three senses of 'universal' when predicated of animal, with an analogy to whiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the natural, logical, and rational universal in any predication and identify which one the logician studies.

Core Idea
A single predicate like 'animal' carries three distinct concepts: essence, universality, and their composite.

When you say 'animal is universal', you are actually combining three separate concepts in your mind. This lesson, drawn from the classical commentary tradition, unpacks these three layers using the analogy of whiteness and a garment. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise definitions in logic and Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three concepts from the passage
Find the three things the author lists when 'animal is universal' is said: the animal in itself, the concept of the universal, and the universal animal which is their composite.
The Arabic terms are: الحيوان من حيث هو هو, مفهوم الكلي, الحيوان الكلي.
2
Understand the distinction proof
The author proves they are distinct because if any two were identical, understanding one would necessitate understanding the other – but we can conceive animal without conceiving universality.
This is a test of conceptual independence.
3
Apply the analogy with whiteness
Just as 'white' is an attribute distinct from 'garment', and 'white garment' is their composite, so 'universal' is an attribute distinct from 'animal', and 'universal animal' is their composite.
The analogy clarifies the subject-attribute relationship.
4
Name the three types
The three are: natural universal (the subject itself, existing externally), logical universal (the attribute of universality, studied by logicians), and rational universal (the composite, existing only in the mind).
Memorize the Arabic terms: كلي طبيعي, كلي منطقي, كلي عقلي.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : إذا قلنا : «الحيوان - مثلاً - كلّي» فهناك أمور ثلاثة : الحيوان من حيث هو هو، ومفهوم الكلّي من غير إشارة إلى مادة من المواد، والحيوان الكلّي وهو المجموع المركب منهما - أي من الحيوان والكلي. والتغاير بين هذه المفهومات ظاهر، فإنه لو كان المفهوم من أحدهما ) عين المفهوم من الآخر لزم من تعقل أحدهما تعقل الآخر؛ وليس كذلك، فإن مفهوم الكلّي مالا يمنع نفس تصوره عن وقوع الشركة فـيـه»، (١) أي الحيوان والكلّي، فإنه إذا ظهر التغاير بين مفهوميهما ظهر التغاير بين كلّ منهما وبين المجموع المركب منهما أيضاً. والحاصل أن مفهوم الحيوان - أعني الجوهر القابل للأبعاد النامي الحساس المتحرك بالإرادة - أمر يعرضه في العقل حالة اعتبارية هي كونه غير مانع من الشركة، فنسبة هذا العارض المسمّى بالكلية إلى ذلك المعروض في العقل كنسبة البياض العارض للثوب في الخارج إليه، فإذا اشتق من البياض «الأبيض» المحمول بالمواطأة على الثوب كان هناك معروض هو «الثوب» وعارض هو مفهوم «الأبيض» ومجموع المركب من المعروض والعارض ؛ كذلك إذا اشتق من الكلية الكلّيّ المحمول بالمواطأة على الحيوان، كان هناك أيضاً معروض هو مفهوم الحيوان» وعارض هو مفهوم الكلي» ومجموع المركب من المعروض والعارض. – وكما أن مفهوم «الأبيض» من حيث هو ليس عين مفهوم «الشوب» ولا جزء له - بل هو مفهوم وخارج هو مفهوم وخارج عنه صالح لأن يحمل على الثوب وعلى غيره - كذلك مفهوم الكلي» ليس عين مفهوم «الحيوان» ولاجزء له، بل مفهوم خارج عنه صالح لأن يحمل على الحيوان وعلى غيره من المفهومات التي تعرضها الكلية في العقل (شريف). ومفهوم الحيوان الجسم النامي الحسّاس المتحرك بالإرادة»، ومن البين جواز تعقل أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر فالأول" يسمّى «كليا طبيعياً» لأنه طبيعة من الطبائع أولأنه موجود في الطبيعة - أي في الخارج. والثاني «كليّاً منطقياً» لأن المنطقي إنما يبحث عنه ؛ وما قاله " المصنف - أن الكلّي المنطقي - كونه كلّيّاً فيه مساهلة، إذ الكلية إنما هي مبدوه والثالث «كلياً عقلياً» لعدم تحققه إلا في العقل.
Statement: When we say 'the animal - for example - is universal', then there are three things: the animal in itself, the concept of the universal without reference to any particular matter, and the universal animal which is the composite of the two - i.e., of animal and universal. The distinction between these concepts is evident, for if the concept of one were identical to the concept of the other, then understanding one would necessitate understanding the other; but it is not so, for the concept of the universal is 'that whose very conception does not prevent participation in it'. (1) i.e., animal and universal, for if the distinction between their two concepts becomes apparent, the distinction between each of them and the composite of the two also becomes apparent. The gist is that the concept of animal - namely, a substance capable of dimensions, growing, sentient, moving by will - has an accidental state in the mind which is its being not preventing participation. So the relation of this attribute called universality to that subject in the mind is like the relation of whiteness occurring to a garment in external existence to it. Thus when from whiteness is derived 'white' predicated by mutual agreement on the garment, there is a subject which is 'garment', an attribute which is the concept of 'white', and a composite of subject and attribute; likewise when from universality is derived 'universal' predicated by mutual agreement on animal, there is also a subject which is the concept of 'animal', an attribute which is the concept of 'universal', and a composite of subject and attribute. – And just as the concept of 'white' in itself is neither identical to the concept of 'garment' nor a part of it - rather it is a concept external to it, suitable to be predicated of the garment and of other things - so too the concept of 'universal' is neither identical to the concept of 'animal' nor a part of it, but rather a concept external to it, suitable to be predicated of animal and of other concepts which are subject to universality in the mind (Sharif). And the concept of animal is 'body growing, sentient, moving by will', and it is clear that it is possible to conceive one while oblivious of the other. So the first is called 'natural universal' because it is a nature among natures or because it exists in nature - i.e., in external existence. The second is called 'logical universal' because the logician investigates it; and the author's statement that the logical universal is 'being universal' is a simplification, since universality is its origin. And the third is called 'rational universal' because it does not exist except in the mind.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion explaining the three aspects of a universal when predicated of a specific subject like 'animal'. The author begins by stating that when we say 'animal is universal', three distinct concepts are involved: (1) the animal itself, considered in its own essence (al-hayawan min haythu huwa huwa), (2) the concept of universality abstracted from any particular matter (mafhum al-kulli min ghayr ishara ila madda), and (3) the composite of the two, i.e., 'the universal animal' (al-hayawan al-kulli). The distinction is proven by the fact that one can conceive animal without conceiving the universal, and vice versa. The author then clarifies the nature of universality as an accidental attribute (arad) in the mind, analogous to whiteness occurring to a garment in external existence. Just as from whiteness we derive the word 'white' which is predicated by mutual agreement (bi al-muwata'a) on the garment, so from universality we derive 'universal' predicated on animal. In both cases we have: a subject (ma'rud), an attribute (arid), and their composite. The author then explicitly names the three types: the first, the subject itself (animal), is called the natural universal (kulliy tabi'i) because it exists in nature; the second, the attribute (concept of universal), is called the logical universal (kulliy mantiqi) because the logician studies it; the third, the composite, is called the rational universal (kulliy 'aqli) because it exists only in the mind. The commentary notes a simplification in the author's statement about the logical universal. The entire discussion uses the example of animal, a subject well known in the Islamic sciences (as used in jurisprudence for analogical reasoning).

كلي منطقي
كلي عقلي
عارض
معروض

How the Natural Universal Differs from the Natural Genus: A Refinement
Commentator refines the definition of natural universal by addressing an objection about its equivalence with natural genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between the natural universal and the natural genus using the concept of suitability (salahiyya).

Core Idea
The natural universal is the essence considered as suitable for universality, not the bare essence.

In the study of the five universals, one might think that the natural universal and the natural genus are the same because both refer to the same essence like 'animal'. This lesson addresses an objection that collapses them and provides the critical refinement: the natural universal considers the essence as a subject suitable for universality, while the natural genus considers it as suitable for genus. Understanding this nuance is essential for precise definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that if 'animal in itself' is a natural universal, then when we say 'animal is a genus', the same thing would be a natural genus, making them identical. Locate this in the Arabic: 'فلا فرق إذن بين مفهوم الكلّي الطبيعي ومفهوم الجنس الطبيعي'.
This objection is based on assuming the essence alone defines both.
2
Analyze the response
The commentator responds that the natural universal is not the bare essence, but the essence considered as a subject (ma'rud) suitable (salahiyya) for the attribute of universality. Find the phrase 'صلاحية العارض مع المعروض'.
The key move is introducing the condition of suitability.
3
Distinguish natural universal from natural genus
The same essence (e.g., animal) can be suitable for different attributes: universality for the natural universal, genus for the natural genus. They differ by the attribute considered, not the subject.
Thus, no logical conflation occurs.
4
Contrast with the rational universal
In the rational universal, the attribute is taken as a condition (qayd) of the subject, not just suitability. This further prevents identity between natural and rational universals.
The rational universal is a composite, while the natural is simple with suitability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١) يعني مفهوم الحيوان من حيث هو هو كلّي طبيعي. قيل عليه : إذا كان مفهوم الحيوان من حيث هو كليّاً طبيعياً، فعلى هذا القياس إذا قلت «الحيوان (جنس كان مفهوم الحيوان من حيث هو جنساً طبيعياً، فلا فرق إذن بين مفهوم الكلّي الطبيعي ومفهوم الجنس الطبيعي. فالصواب أن مفهوم الحيوان من حيث هو معروض لمفهوم الكلّي أو صالح لكونه معروضاً له كلي طبيعي، ومن حيث هو معروض لمفهوم الجنس أو صالح لكونه معروضاً له جنس طبيعي، فقد اعتبر في الطبيعي صلاحية العارض مع المعروض فلا إشكال حينئذ ، وإذا اعتبر العارض معه بطريق القيدية دون الجزئية كما في العقلي فلا يلزم اتحاد الطبيعي والعقلي أيضاً (شريف). (٢) يعني أنه يأخذ مفهوم الكلّي من حيث هو هو ، بلا إشارة إلى مادة مخصوصة، ويورد عليه أحكاماً لتكون تلك الأحكام عامة شاملة لجميع ما يصدق عليه مفهوم الكلي (شريف). (٣) أي مبدء الكلي، وأراد بالمبدء المشتق منه، فإن نسبة الكلية إلى الكلي كنسبة الضرب والضاربية إلى الضارب (شريف).
(1) He means the concept of animal in itself is a natural universal. It was objected: If the concept of animal in itself is a natural universal, then by this analogy, when you say 'animal is a genus', the concept of animal in itself would be a natural genus; thus there would be no difference between the concept of natural universal and the concept of natural genus. The correct view is that the concept of animal in itself is a subject for the concept of the universal or is suitable to be a subject for it, so it is a natural universal; and in itself it is a subject for the concept of genus or suitable to be a subject for it, so it is a natural genus. Thus in the natural, the suitability of the attribute with the subject is considered, so there is no problem then. And if the attribute is considered with it in the manner of being a condition (qaydiyya) rather than as a part (juz'iyya), as in the rational universal, then no unification of the natural and the rational occurs either (Sharif). (2) He means he takes the concept of the universal in itself, without reference to a particular matter, and applies judgments to it so that those judgments are general, covering everything to which the concept of the universal applies (Sharif). (3) i.e., the origin of the universal, and by 'origin' he means that from which it is derived, for the relation of universality to the universal is like the relation of hitting (al-darb) and the ability to hit (al-daribiyya) to the hitter (al-darib) (Sharif).
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hachiya) addressing an objection against the definition of the natural universal. The commentator (Sharif) explains the objection: If the concept of animal in itself (min haythu huwa huwa) is considered the natural universal, then by extension, when we say 'animal is a genus', the same concept would be a natural genus. This would collapse the distinction between the natural universal and the natural genus, both being the same essence. The commentator responds by clarifying that the natural universal is not the bare essence, but the essence considered as a subject (ma'rud) suitable for the attribute of universality. Similarly, the natural genus is the same essence considered as a subject suitable for the attribute of genus. Thus, the natural universal and natural genus are not identical in concept because they involve different attributes (universality vs. genus) even though the underlying subject is the same. Furthermore, the rational universal (the composite) is not the same as the natural universal because in the rational, the attribute is considered as a condition (qayd) of the subject, not as a part (juz'). This avoids any unification. The passage also includes two additional glosses: one clarifying that the logical universal is taken abstractly to apply general judgments, and another explaining that 'origin' (mabda') means the source from which the term is derived, like 'hitting' is the origin of 'hitter'. This refinement guards against the misunderstanding that the subject itself (animal) is inherently universal or generic without considering its suitability for attributes.

Key Terms
صلاحية
قيدية
مبدء

The natural universal and natural genus are distinguished by the different attributes they are suitable to receive, not by the subject essence.

The Five Universals

The Three Types of Universal: Natural, Logical, and Rational
The three types of universal and their distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of universal and explain the difference between a natural universal and a natural genus.

Core Idea
The same concept can be natural, logical, or rational depending on how it is considered.

When we say 'animal', we can think of it in three different ways: as a nature existing in the real world, as a concept predicated of many species, or as a mental abstraction. These are the three types of universal. The passage also clarifies a common confusion: the natural universal is not identical to the natural genus; rather, it is the concept as apt for universality. Let's unpack this important distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example used
The passage uses 'animal' as the running example. Note how the same concept is examined under three aspects.
2
Define the natural universal
The natural universal is the concept considered in itself as a nature existing in the external world. For example, the nature 'animal' as found in individual animals.
It is called 'natural' because it exists in nature (the external world).
3
Define the logical universal
The logical universal is the concept considered as a predicate applicable to many things. It is the subject matter of logic, studied without regard to specific content.
The author notes that calling this 'universal' is somewhat lenient; universality is derived from it.
4
Define the rational universal
The rational universal is the concept as it exists only in the mind, abstracted from particular instances. It has no external existence.
5
Distinguish natural universal from natural genus
The passage addresses an objection: if 'animal as such' is a natural universal, then 'animal as genus' would also be natural, leading to confusion. The refinement is that the natural universal is the concept considered as subject to (or apt for) universality, while the natural genus is the concept considered as subject to the notion of genus. They are different relations.
This avoids equating the two concepts. The natural universal is not the same as the natural genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومفهوم الحيوان الجسم النامي الحسّاس المتحرك بالإرادة»، ومن البين جواز تعقل أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر فالأول يسمّى «كليا طبيعياً» لأنه طبيعة من الطبائع أو لأنه موجود في الطبيعة - أي في الخارج. والثاني «كليّاً منطقياً» لأن المنطقي إنما يبحث عنه ؛ وما قاله المصنف - أن الكلّي المنطقي - كونه كلّيّاً فيه مساهلة، إذ الكلية إنما هي مبدوه. والثالث «كليّاً عقلياً» لعدم تحققه إلا في العقل. (١) يعني مفهوم الحيوان من حيث هو هو كلّي طبيعي. قيل عليه : إذا كان مفهوم الحيوان من حيث هو كليّاً طبيعياً، فعلى هذا القياس إذا قلت «الحيوان (جنس) كان مفهوم الحيوان من حيث هو جنساً طبيعياً، فلا فرق إذن بين مفهوم الكلّي الطبيعي ومفهوم الجنس الطبيعي. فالصواب أن مفهوم الحيوان من حيث هو معروض لمفهوم الكلّي أو صالح لكونه معروضاً له كلي طبيعي، ومن حيث هو معروض لمفهوم الجنس أو صالح لكونه معروضاً له جنس طبيعي، فقد اعتبر في الطبيعي صلاحية العارض مع المعروض فلا إشكال حينئذ ، وإذا اعتبر العارض معه بطريق القيدية دون الجزئية كما في العقلي فلا يلزم اتحاد الطبيعي والعقلي أيضاً (شريف). (٢) يعني أنه يأخذ مفهوم الكلّي من حيث هو هو ، بلا إشارة إلى مادة مخصوصة، ويورد عليه أحكاماً لتكون تلك الأحكام عامة شاملة لجميع ما يصدق عليه مفهوم الكلي (شريف). (٣) أي مبدء الكلي، وأراد بالمبدء المشتق منه، فإن نسبة الكلية إلى الكلي كنسبة الضرب والضاربية إلى الضارب (شريف). وإنما قال الحيوان مثلاً» لأنّ اعتبار هذه الأمور الثلاثة لا يختص بالحيوان ولا بمفهوم الكلّي، بل يتناول سائر الماهيات ومفهومات الكليات حتى إذا قلنا : الإنسان نوع حصل عندنا نوع طبيعي ونوع منطقي ونوع عقلي؛ وكذلك في الجنس والفصل وغيرهما. والكلّي الطبيعي موجود في الخارج، لأن هذا الحيوان موجود، و«الحيوان» جزء من هذا الحيوان الموجود، وجزء الموجود موجود، فالحيوان موجود ؛ وهو الكلي الطبيعي. وأما الكليان الآخران - أي الكلّي المنطقي والكلي العقلي – ففي وجودهما في الخارج خلاف ؛ والنظر في ذلك خارج عن الصناعة، لأنه من مسائل الحكمة الإلهية الباحثة عن أحوال الموجودات من حيث أنه موجود، وهذا مشترك بينهما وبين الكلي الطبيعي، فلاوجه لإيراده هاهنا وإحالتهما على علم آخر (شریف).
And the concept of animal is 'the growing, sensing, voluntarily moving body', and it is clear that it is permissible to conceive one of them while heedless of the other. The first is called 'natural universal' because it is a nature among natures or because it exists in nature—i.e., in the external world. The second is 'logical universal' because the logician investigates it; and what the author said—that the logical universal is universal—involves some lenience, for universality is derived from it. The third is 'rational universal' because it is not realized except in the mind. (1) He means the concept of animal as such is a natural universal. It was objected: if the concept of animal as such is a natural universal, then by this analogy when you say 'animal (genus)', the concept of animal as a genus would be a natural genus, so there would be no difference between the concept of natural universal and the concept of natural genus. The correct view is that the concept of animal as subject to the concept of universal or apt to be subject to it is a natural universal, and as subject to the concept of genus or apt to be subject to it is a natural genus. Thus in the natural [universal], the suitability of the accident along with the subject is considered, so then there is no problem. And if the accident is considered with it by way of conditionality rather than as a part, as in the rational [universal], then the unification of the natural and the rational also does not follow (Sharif). (2) He means that [the logician] takes the concept of universal as such, without reference to a specific matter, and imposes upon it rulings so that those rulings are general, encompassing everything to which the concept of universal applies (Sharif). (3) i.e., the source of the universal, and by 'source' he means that from which it is derived, for the relation of universality to the universal is like the relation of 'beating' and 'beatenness' to the beater (Sharif). And he said 'animal' as an example only, because the consideration of these three matters is not specific to animal nor to the concept of universal; rather, it encompasses all essences and concepts of universals, so that if we say 'man is a species', we obtain a natural species, a logical species, and a rational species; and likewise in genus, differentia, and others. The natural universal is existent in the external world, because this animal is existent, and 'animal' is a part of this existent animal, and a part of the existent is existent; so animal is existent, and it is the natural universal. As for the other two universals—i.e., the logical universal and the rational universal—there is disagreement about their existence in the external world; and the consideration of that is outside the discipline, because it belongs to the questions of divine wisdom which investigates the states of existents insofar as they are existent, and this is common between them and the natural universal. Thus there is no reason to mention it here, and we relegate it to another science (Sharif).
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed definition of the three types of universal: natural, logical, and rational. The author uses the concept 'animal' as an example to illustrate how one and the same concept can be considered under three distinct aspects. The natural universal is the concept considered in itself, as it exists in external reality; it is a nature found in the world. The logical universal is the concept considered as a predicate that can be applied to many things; it is the subject matter of logic. The rational universal is the concept as it exists in the mind, abstracted from particular instances. The passage includes a subtle refinement: the natural universal, such as 'animal', is not identical to the natural genus; rather, it is the concept insofar as it is apt to be subject to the notion of universality, while the genus is a different relational aspect. This avoids confusion between the natural universal and natural genus. The author also provides a brief argument for the existence of the natural universal in the external world: a particular animal exists, and 'animal' is a part of that existent, so 'animal' exists. However, the existence of the logical and rational universals is a matter of debate and belongs to metaphysics (divine wisdom), not logic proper. Therefore, the discussion of their existence is set aside.

Key Terms
كلي طبيعي
كلي منطقي
كلي عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The concept 'animal' as a nature existing in individual animals.
D) The concept 'animal' as a term in a logical proposition.

Key Takeaway
The three types of universal (natural, logical, rational) reflect the same concept viewed as external reality, predictable notion, and mental abstraction, with the natural universal distinct from the natural genus.

The Five Universals
