The Four Relations Among Universals and Their Domain
Author defines the four relations between universals and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four relations between universals (equality, absolute generality/specificity, generality/specificity from one aspect, disparity) and explain why impossible universals are excluded from this classification.

Core Idea
The four relations are exhaustive only for universals that actually have instances.

When comparing two universals, we ask: do they share any individuals? This passage by al-Qutbi defines the four possible relations: from full overlap (equality) to no overlap (disparity). The commentator then addresses an important edge case: what about universals like 'nothing' that have no instances? The answer refines the classification to apply only to universals that are true of something in actuality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four relation types
From the Arabic, list the four relations: التساوي (tasāwī), العموم والخصوص المطلق (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq), العموم والخصوص من وجه (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh), and التباين (tabāyun). Write their definitions in your own words.
Each relation is defined by the truth of one universal over the individuals of the other.
2
Extract the logical conditions
For each relation, extract the specific condition using the phrase 'صدق على' (true of). For equality: each is true of every individual the other is true of. For absolute: one is true of all individuals of the other but not vice versa. For partial: each is true of some individuals of the other but not all. For disparity: neither is true of any individual of the other.
Use the examples to verify the conditions.
3
Apply the conditions to the examples
Check that the given examples satisfy the conditions: human/natiq (equality), animal/human (absolute), animal/white (partial), human/horse (disparity).
Also test with other pairs like 'movement' and 'black' to internalize the logic.
4
Understand the objection
The objection points to universals like 'nothing' (اللاشيء) and 'impossible' (اللاممكن بالإمكان العام) that have no instances at all. According to the original definitions, they would be considered disparate, but their contradictories 'thing' and 'possible' are actually equal, leading to a contradiction.
This shows the classification needs a restriction.
5
See the resolution
The answer restricts the classification to universals that are true of something in actuality (يصدق في نفس الأمر على شيء) or can be. Hypothetical universals (الكليات الفرضية) that cannot be true of anything are excluded. The rules apply only according to human capacity and the purposes of logic.
This preserves the exhaustiveness for the intended domain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القال : الثالث الكلّيّان متساويان، إن صدق كل واحد منهما على كل ما يصدق عليه الآخر، كالإنسان والناطق. وبينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق، إن صدق أحدهما على كل ما يصدق عليه الآخر من غير عكس، كالحيوان والإنسان. وبينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه إن صدق كل منهما على بعض ما صدق عليه الآخر فقط، كالحيوان والأبيض. ومتباينان إن لم يصدق شيء منهما على شيء كما يصدق عليه الآخر كالإنسان والفرس.
أقول : النسب بين الكلّيين منحصرة في أربعة : التساوي، والعموم والخصوص المطلق، والعموم والخصوص من وجه، والتباين. وذلك لأنّ الكلّي إذا نسب إلى كلّي آخر، فإما أن يصدقا على شيء واحد أو لم يصدقا، فإن لم يصدقا على شيء أصلاً، فهما «متباينان»، كالإنسان والفرس، فإنّه لا يصدق الإنسان على شيء من أفراد الفرس وبالعكس. وإن صدقا على شيء : فلا يخلوا إما أن يصدق كل منهما على كل ما يصدق عليه الآخر أو لا يصدق : فإن صدقا فهما «متساويان» كالإنسان والناطق، فإنّ كلّ ما يصدق عليه الإنسان يصدق عليه الناطق وبالعكس. وإن لم يصدق : فإما أن يصدق أحدهما على كل ما صدق عليه الآخر من غير عكس أو لا يصدق، فإن صدق كان بينهما «عموم وخصوص مطلق» والصادق على كل ما صدق عليه الآخر أعم مطلقاً، والآخر أخص.
(۱) اعترض عليه بأن اللاشيء واللاممكن بالإمكان العام لا يصدقان على شيء أصلاً - لا في الخارج ولا في الذهن - فإن جعلا متباينين وجب أن يكون بين نقيضيهما تباين جزئي - على ماسيأتي - وهو باطل، لأن «الشيء» و «الممكن العام» متساويان ؛ وإن لم يجعلا من المتباينين : فقد دخل في تعريفهما ماليس منهما. وأجيب بتخصيص الدعوى بالكليات الصادقة في نفس الأمر على شيء أو أشياء، أو التي يمكن صدقها كذلك، فيخرج الكليات الفرضية التي يمتنع صدقها في نفس الأمر على شيء من الأشياء خارجاً وذهناً ؛ فكأنه قيل : «الكليان اللذان يصدق كل منهما على شيء بحسب نفس الأمر ينحصران في الأقسام الأربعة» وتعميم القواعد إنما يجب بحسب الطاقة البشرية وبحسب الأغراض المطلوبة من الفنّ، ولا غرض لهم (المنطقي) في الكليات الفرضية، بل في الكليات الموجودة أصالة، أو الصادقة في نفس الأمر على شيء تبعاً، ولا يمكن أيضاً إدراجها في هذه الأقسام مع رعاية تلك الأحكام (شريف).
The author said: The third [section]: Two universals are equal if each is true of everything that the other is true of, like 'human' and 'rational animal'. Between them is absolute generality and specificity if one is true of everything that the other is true of without the reverse, like 'animal' and 'human'. Between them is generality and specificity from one aspect if each is true of only some of what the other is true of, like 'animal' and 'white'. And they are disparate if neither is true of anything that the other is true of, like 'human' and 'horse'. I say: The relations between universals are confined to four: equality, absolute generality and specificity, generality and specificity from one aspect, and disparity. That is because when a universal is related to another universal, either they are true of a single thing or they are not. If they are not true of anything at all, then they are 'disparate', like 'human' and 'horse', for 'human' is not true of any individual of 'horse' and vice versa. If they are true of a thing, then either each is true of everything the other is true of or not. If they are true, then they are 'equal', like 'human' and 'rational animal', for everything that 'human' is true of, 'rational animal' is true of and vice versa. If they are not [each true of everything], then either one of them is true of everything the other is true of without the reverse, or not. If it is true, then between them is 'absolute generality and specificity', and the one true of everything the other is true of is absolutely more general, and the other is more specific. (1) An objection was raised against this: that 'nothing' and 'the non-possible in the general sense of possibility' are not true of anything at all – neither in the external world nor in the mind. If they are made disparate, then there would be partial disparity between their contradictories – as will come – which is false, because 'thing' and 'the possible in general' are equal. If they are not made among the disparate, then what is not of them enters into their definition. It was answered by restricting the claim to universals that are true in actuality of something or things, or that can be true likewise. Thus, the hypothetical universals whose truth is impossible in actuality on any of the things externally and mentally are excluded. It is as if it was said: 'The two universals each of which is true of something according to actuality are limited to the four divisions.' Generalizing the rules is only necessary according to human capacity and according to the purposes sought from the discipline. The logicians have no purpose in hypothetical universals, but rather in universals that exist primarily, or that are true in actuality of something secondarily. It is also not possible to include them in these divisions while observing those rulings. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage defines the four relations between universals, a key topic in the logic of terms. The author begins by stating the definitions: equality (each is true of everything the other is true of), absolute generality/specificity (one is true of everything the other is true of but not vice versa), generality/specificity from one aspect (each is true of some of what the other is true of but not all), and disparity (neither is true of anything the other is true of). Examples are given from the animal kingdom and rational faculty. The commentator then expands: the relations are exhaustive because when comparing two universals, either they have no common subject (disparity) or they do. If they have common subjects, then either they coincide perfectly (equality) or they do not. If they do not coincide perfectly, then either one is fully contained in the other (absolute) or there is partial overlap (from one aspect). This logical division is exhaustive. The passage then addresses an objection: what about concepts like 'nothing' or 'impossible' that have no instances at all? According to the definitions, they would be considered disparate? But their contradictories ('thing' and 'possible') are actually equal. The objection shows a problematic edge case. The answer restricts the definitions to universals that actually have instances (are true of something in actuality) or could have instances. Hypothetical or impossible universals are excluded because they are not the concern of logic. This refinement ensures the definitions are practically applicable within the scope of the discipline. The commentator (Sharif) notes that general rules are made according to human capacity and the purpose of the art.

Key Terms
التساوي
العموم والخصوص المطلق
العموم والخصوص من وجه
التباين
الكليات الفرضية

Key Takeaway
The four relations between universals are exhaustive only for universals that actually have instances; impossible universals are irrelevant to logical analysis.

Truth Condition for Equality and Generality of Universals
Commentator clarifies truth condition for equality and generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why equality of two universals does not require simultaneous instantiation, using the sleeper/wakeful example and the phrase 'in general' (fi al-jumla).

Core Idea
Equality of universals is about cross-application across individuals, not coincident truth at a single moment.

In this passage, the commentator Sharif addresses a subtle but crucial point: when two universals are said to be equal, what does it mean for them to be 'true of each other's individuals'? He shows that it does not require them to be true together at the same time. Using the everyday example of sleeper and wakeful, you will see how a general ('in general') application resolves the apparent contradiction. This clarifies the criterion for equality and absolute generality, preventing a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations discussed
The passage addresses the truth condition for equality (tasāwī) and absolute generality (ʿumūm muṭlaq). Understand that both relations involve one universal applying to all individuals of the other.
2
Recognize the core claim
The criterion is that each universal is true of all individuals of the other. The commentator emphasizes that this does NOT require them to be true together (maʿan) in one subject simultaneously.
The phrase 'yasdaqāni maʿan fī wāḥid' is explicitly denied as a requirement.
3
Analyze the sleeper/wakeful example
Sleeper and wakeful are considered equal despite being mutually exclusive at any given moment. Every sleeper is wakeful in a general sense (because he wakes), and every wakeful is a sleeper in general (because he sleeps at other times).
The example illustrates that equality holds across time, not at a single instant.
4
Understand the resolution via 'in general'
The phrase 'fī al-jumla' (in general) means each universal applies to the individuals of the other at the time the other applies to them. The sleeper at the time of sleeping is truthfully called 'wakeful in general' because he will wake.
This temporal qualification resolves the apparent contradiction.
5
Generalize to other relations
The same principle applies to absolute generality and partial generality (ʿumūm min wajh). The truth condition is always considered in a temporal or general sense, not requiring simultaneous co-instantiation.
This prevents a narrow interpretation that would invalidate many legitimate definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتبر فيهما صدق كل منهما على جميع أفراد الآخر ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يصدقا معاً في واحد، فإن النائم والمستيقظ متساويان مع امتناع اجتماعهما في زمان واحد، وربما يقال : التساوي إنما هو بين النائم في الجملة والمستيقظ في الجملة فالنائم في حال نومه يصدق عليه أنه «مستيقظ في الجملة» وإن لم يصدق عليه أنه «مستيقظ في حال النوم» وكذا المستيقظ يصدق عليه في حال يقظته أنه «نائم في الجملة» فالمتساويان يصدق كل منهما على جميع أفراد الآخر في زمان صدق الآخر عليه ؛ وقس على ذلك الصدق المعتبر في العموم مطلقاً والعموم من وجه (شريف).
The criterion in them is that each is true of all individuals of the other, but it does not follow from that that they are true together in one [subject]. For example, the sleeper and the wakeful are equal despite the impossibility of their being together at one time. And perhaps it is said: Equality is only between the sleeper in general and the wakeful in general, for the sleeper in his sleep is truthfully described as 'wakeful in general' even if he is not described as 'wakeful while asleep'. Similarly, the wakeful in his wakefulness is truthfully described as 'sleeper in general'. So the two equals are each true of all individuals of the other in the time that the other is true of them. Analogize that to the truth considered in absolute generality and generality from one aspect. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This short passage addresses a nuanced point about the truth condition in the relations of equality and absolute generality. The commentator clarifies that for two universals to be equal (or one general and the other specific), it is required that each is true of all individuals of the other, but it is not required that they be simultaneously true of the same individual at the same time. The example of 'sleeper' and 'wakeful' illustrates this: these are considered equal because every sleeper is wakeful in a general sense (i.e., at some time) and every wakeful is a sleeper in a general sense, even though they cannot be true together at the same moment. This shows that the truth condition is considered across individuals in the temporal dimension: each universal applies to the individuals of the other at the time the other applies. The same principle applies to absolute generality and partial generality. This refinement prevents misunderstanding that might arise from thinking that equality requires co-instantiation at the same instant.

Key Terms
يصدقان معاً في واحد
في الجملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) They have absolute generality and specificity.
D) They have generality and specificity from one aspect.

Key Takeaway
The truth condition for equality of universals is that each applies to all individuals of the other across time, not necessarily at the same instant.

The Five Universals

The Four Relations Between Universal Terms
Author enumerates and defines the four relations between universal terms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four relations between universal terms—disparity, equality, absolute generality, and generality from one aspect—and express each in the corresponding categorical proposition forms.

Core Idea
Every pair of universal terms has one of four relations; these relations are the key to understanding how concepts overlap or exclude each other.

In logic, when two universal terms are considered, they can relate in exactly one of four ways. The commentator here lays out these four relations with clear examples, which forms the foundation for more complex logical operations like syllogisms and definitions. By mastering these relations, you will be able to analyze the logical structure of any pair of universal concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify disparity (تباين)
Find when two universals share no individuals at all. The passage gives 'human' and 'horse' as an example. Note the propositional form: two universal negatives (e.g., 'No human is a horse' and vice versa).
Disparity is the simplest relation—complete exclusion.
2
Identify equality (تساوي)
Find when each universal applies to all individuals of the other. Example: 'human' and 'rational'. The passage clarifies that equality does not require simultaneous truth in the same subject; 'sleeping' and 'awake' are coextensive in a time-sensitive sense.
The propositional form is two universal affirmatives.
3
Identify absolute generality (عموم وخصوص مطلق)
Find when one universal applies to all individuals of the other but not vice versa. Example: 'human' and 'animal'. The more general term (animal) covers the more specific (human) and more.
Propositional form: one universal affirmative and one particular negative.
4
Identify generality from one aspect (عموم وخصوص من وجه)
Find when each universal applies to some individuals the other does not, and they share some individuals. Example: 'animal' and 'white'. There are three subcases: common, exclusive to first, exclusive to second.
Propositional form: two particular negatives and one particular affirmative.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالإنسان والفرس، فإنّه لا يصدق الإنسان على شيء من أفراد الفرس وبالعكس. وإن صدقا على شيء : فلا يخلوا إما أن يصدق كل منهما على كل ما يصدق عليه الآخر أو لا يصدق : فإن صدقا فهما «متساويان» كالإنسان والناطق، فإنّ كلّ ما يصدق عليه الإنسان يصدق عليه الناطق وبالعكس. (1) المعتبر فيهما صدق كل منهما على جميع أفراد الآخر ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يصدقا معاً في واحد، فإن النائم والمستيقظ متساويان مع امتناع اجتماعهما في زمان واحد، وربما يقال : التساوي إنما هو بين النائم في الجملة والمستيقظ في الجملة فالنائم في حال نومه يصدق عليه أنه «مستقيظ في الجملة» وإن لم يصدق عليه أنه «مستيقظ في حال النوم وكذا المستيقظ يصدق عليه في حال يقظته أنه «نائم في الجملة» فالمتساويان يصدق كل منهما على جميع أفراد الآخر في زمان صدق الآخر عليه ؛ وقس على ذلك الصدق المعتبر في العموم مطلقاً والعموم من وجه (شريف). وإن لم يصدق : فإما أن يصدق أحدهما على كل ما صدق عليه الآخر من غير عكس أو لا يصدق، فإن صدق كان بينهما «عموم وخصوص مطلق» والصادق على كل ما صدق عليه الآخر أعم مطلقاً، والآخر أخص مطلقاً كالإنسان والحيوان، فإن الإنسان حيوان وليس كل حيوان إنساناً. وإن لم يصدق كان بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه»، وكل واحد منهما أعم من الآخر من وجه وأخص من وجه، فإنهما لما صدقا على شيء ولم يصدق أحدهما على كلّ ما صدق عليه الآخر كان هناك ثلاث صور: إحداها ما يجتمعان فيها على الصدق، والثانية ما يصدق فيها هذا دون ذاك، والثالثة ما يصدق فيها ذاك دون هذا - كالحيوان والأبيض فإنهما يصدقان معاً على الحيوان الأبيض، ويصدق الحيوان بدون الأبيض على الحيوان الأسود؛ وبالعكس في الجماد الأبيض – فيكون كل واحد منهما شاملاً للآخر وغيره، فالحيوان شامل للأبيض وغير الأبيض، والأبيض شامل للحيوان وغير الحيوان؛ فباعتبار أن كل واحد منهما شامل للآخر وغيره يكون أعم منه ؛ وباعتبار أنه مشمول له يكون أخص منه. فمرجع التباين إلى سالبتين كليتين من الطرفين، كقولنا «لاشيء تما هو إنسان فهو فرس» و «لاشيء تما هو فرس فهو إنسان». والتساوي إلى موجبتين كلّيّتين، كقولنا : «كل ما هو إنسان فهو ناطق» و «كل ما هو ناطق فهو إنسان». والعموم المطلق إلى موجبة كلّيّة من أحد الطرفين وسالبة جزئية من الطرف الآخر، كقولنا كل ما هو إنسان فهو حيوان» و «ليس بعض ما هو حيوان فهو إنسان». والعموم من وجه إلى سالبتين جزئيتين وموجبة جزئية، كقولنا :
Like 'human' and 'horse', for 'human' is not predicated of any individual of 'horse' and vice versa. And if they are both predicated of something, then either each is predicated of everything the other is predicated of, or not. If they are, then they are 'coextensive' like 'human' and 'rational', for everything 'human' is predicated of, 'rational' is predicated of, and vice versa. (1) What is considered in them is the predication of each of all individuals of the other, and it does not follow from that that they are both true of one thing at once, for 'sleeping' and 'awake' are coextensive with the impossibility of their coexistence in one time. And it might be said: coextensivity is only between 'sleeping in general' and 'awake in general', so the sleeper in his sleep is truly described as 'awake in general' even if not as 'awake during sleep', and similarly the awake person in his wakefulness is truly described as 'sleeping in general', so the two coextensives are each predicated of all individuals of the other at the time the other is predicated of them. And extend that to the predication considered in absolute generality and generality from one aspect (Sharif). And if they are not both predicated (of everything the other is predicated of), then either one is predicated of everything the other is predicated of without the converse, or not. If it is, then between them is 'absolute generality and specificity', and the one predicated of everything the other is predicated of is absolutely more general, and the other is absolutely more specific, like 'human' and 'animal', for human is an animal but not every animal is human. And if it is not, then between them is 'generality and specificity from one aspect', and each is more general than the other in one aspect and more specific in another aspect. For when they are both predicated of something, and one is not predicated of everything the other is predicated of, there are three cases: one where they both are true together, a second where this one is true without that, and a third where that one is true without this — like 'animal' and 'white', for they are both true of a white animal, and 'animal' is true without 'white' of a black animal, and vice versa for a white inanimate object. So each includes the other and what is other than it. Thus 'animal' includes 'white' and non-white, and 'white' includes 'animal' and non-animal. So insofar as each includes the other and what is other, it is more general than it; and insofar as it is included by it, it is more specific. So disparity reduces to two universal negatives from both sides, as we say 'Nothing that is a human is a horse' and 'Nothing that is a horse is a human'. And coextensivity reduces to two universal affirmatives, as we say 'Every human is rational' and 'Every rational is human'. And absolute generality reduces to a universal affirmative from one side and a particular negative from the other, as we say 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animal is not human'. And generality from one aspect reduces to two particular negatives and a particular affirmative, as we say:
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya defines the four possible relations between universal terms when both are truly predicated of at least one thing. The author begins with the relation of disparity (تباين), where neither universal applies to any individual of the other, using 'human' and 'horse' as an example. Then he addresses cases where the universals share some common subject. The first is equality (تساوي), where each universal applies to all individuals of the other, as with 'human' and 'rational'. A marginal note (attributed to Sharif) clarifies that equality does not require simultaneous truth in one subject, giving the example of 'sleeping' and 'awake'—they are coextensive in the sense that every sleeper is awake in general (at some time) and every awake person is sleeping in general, even though they cannot coexist at the same moment. The second division is absolute generality and specificity (عموم وخصوص مطلق), where one universal applies to all individuals of the other but not vice versa, exemplified by 'human' and 'animal'. The third is generality and specificity from one aspect (عموم وخصوص من وجه), where each universal applies to some individuals the other does not, and they also share some common individuals. The example given is 'animal' and 'white': they both apply to a white animal; 'animal' applies to black animals (where 'white' does not), and 'white' applies to white inanimate objects (where 'animal' does not). Thus each is both more general (includes the other and other things) and more specific (is included by the other). Finally, the author reduces each relation to the categorical propositions that express it: disparity corresponds to two universal negatives, equality to two universal affirmatives, absolute generality to one universal affirmative and one particular negative, and generality from one aspect to two particular negatives and one particular affirmative. This systematic classification is fundamental for understanding predication in logic.

تساوي
عموم وخصوص مطلق
عموم وخصوص من وجه

Why the Four Relations Are Only Between Universals
Author explains why the four logical relations are only between universals, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any pair of terms (universal/universal, particular/particular, or universal/particular) and determine which of the four logical relations apply, explaining why only universals can have all four.

Core Idea
The four logical relations fully apply only between two universals; particulars and mixed pairs are limited.

In Islamic logic, the four relations (equality, total inclusion, mutual inclusion, and mutual exclusion) are foundational for definition and demonstration. This passage explains why logicians restrict these relations to universals, clarifying the limited possibilities for particulars and mixed pairs, and resolves a subtle objection about coinciding descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of concept pairs
The text states that concepts (مفهومان) can be both universal, both particular, or one universal and one particular. Note that only the universal-universal pair can exhibit all four relations.
The classification is based on whether the term is a universal (كلي) or a particular (جزئي).
2
Determine the relations possible for two particulars
Two distinct particulars (e.g., Zayd and 'Amr) are always mutually exclusive (متباينان) because they cannot share a single essence. Thus only one relation—mutual exclusion—applies.
If they were not mutually exclusive, they would be the same particular, which contradicts their distinctness.
3
Determine the relations possible for a universal and a particular
A particular either falls under the universal (making it more specific, i.e., the particular is a subset of the universal) or it does not (mutual exclusion). Hence only two relations exist: inclusion (if the particular is subordinate) or exclusion.
For example, Zayd is a particular of 'human'; 'human' and Zayd have an inclusion relation.
4
Analyze the objection about coinciding descriptions
The objector asks about 'this laughing one' and 'this writer one' as coinciding particulars. The author clarifies that if they refer to different individuals (Zayd and 'Amr), they are mutually exclusive; if to the same individual (Zayd), there is only one true particular with two attributes, not two distinct particulars.
This reinforces that only genuinely distinct particulars are considered, not mere conceptual distinctions.
5
Conclude why universals are the focus
Since only between two universals can all four relations occur, logicians prioritize studying relations among universals. This is the primary purpose (المقصد الأصلي) of the discussion.
Understanding these relations is crucial for constructing definitions (حدود) and syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما اعتبرت النسب بين الكلّيّين دون المفهومين ، لأن المفهومين إما كليان أو جزئيان أو كلّي وجزئي، والنسب الأربع لا تتحقق في القسمين الأخيرين : أما الجزئيان فلأنهما لا يكونان إلا متباينين، وأما الكلي والجزئي فلايوجد فيهما إلا قسمان فقط، وفي الجزئيين إلا قسم واحد فلوقال : «المفهومان المتساويان... - إلى آخر التقسيم - لربما توهم جريان جميع هذه الأقسام الأربعة في كل واحد من الأقسام الثلاثة، فلما قال : «الكليان علم أن ليس حال القسمين الأخيرين كذلك، وإلا لكان التخصيص لغواً. فإن قلت: قد علم مما ذكر عدم جريان النسب الأربع فيهما، لكن لم يعلم ماذا فيهما من تلك النسب ؟ قلت : يعلم ذلك بالمقايسة بأدنى التفات على أن المقصود الأصلي معرفة أحوال النسب الكليات بعضها مع بعض. (شريف) فإن قلت : «هذا الضاحك» و «هذا الكاتب جزئيان متصادقان فلايكونان متباينين. قلت: إن كان المشار إليه بـ «هذا الضاحك» زيداً مثلاً، وبهذا الكاتب عمراً فهناك جزئيان متباينان، وإن كان المشار إليه بهما «زيداً» مثلاً، فليس هناك إلا جزئي حقيقي واحد هو ذات زيد لكنه اعتبر معه تارة اتصافه بالضحك، وأخرى اتصافه بالكتابة، وبذلك لم يتعدّد الجزئي الحقيقي تعدّداً حقيقياً ولم يتغاير حقيقياً، بل هناك تعدّد وتغاير بحسب الاعتبارات، والكلام في الجزئي والكلّيّ فلأن الجزئي إن كان جزئيّاً لذلك الكلّي يكون أخص منه مطلقاً، وإن لم يكن جزئياً له يكون مبايناً له.
And the relations are considered only between the two universals, not between the two concepts, because the two concepts are either both universal, both particular, or one universal and one particular. The four relations are not realized in the last two sections: as for the two particulars, they are only mutually exclusive; and as for the universal and particular, only two sections exist in them, and in the two particulars only one section. For if he said: 'the two co-extensive concepts...' up to the end of the division, it might be imagined that all these four sections occur in each of the three sections. So when he said: 'the two universals', it is known that the case of the last two sections is not like that; otherwise the specification would be pointless. If you say: It is known from what was mentioned that the four relations do not occur in the two [last sections], but what is there of those relations is not known? I say: That is known by comparison with the least attention, while the primary purpose is to know the states of the universal relations with each other. (Sharif) If you say: 'This laughing [one]' and 'this writer [one]' are two particular individuals that coincide, so they are not mutually exclusive? I say: If what is pointed to by 'this laughing one' is Zayd for example, and by 'this writer one' is 'Amr, then there are two mutually exclusive particulars. And if what is pointed to by both is Zayd for example, then there is only one true particular which is the essence of Zayd, but he is considered sometimes with the attribute of laughing and sometimes with the attribute of writing, and by that the true particular does not become multiple truly nor does it differ truly, rather there is multiplicity and difference according to considerations; and the discussion is about the particular and the universal, for the particular if it is a particular of that universal, then it is more specific than it absolutely, and if it is not a particular of it, then it is mutually exclusive with it.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage explains why the four logical relations (equality, total inclusion, mutual inclusion from one aspect, and mutual exclusion) are considered only between universals and not between concepts in general. The author first justifies this by noting that concepts can be universal, particular, or mixed. The four relations only fully apply between two universals. For two particulars, they are always mutually exclusive (since no two distinct particulars share the same essence). For a universal and a particular, only two relations exist: either the particular falls under the universal (making it more specific) or it does not (making them exclusive). The objection about 'this laughing one' and 'this writer one' being coinciding particulars is addressed by distinguishing between true multiplicity of individuals and mere consideration of attributes of a single individual. The example of Zayd and 'Amr shows true mutual exclusion, whereas considering Zayd with different attributes does not produce a second true particular. In Islamic logic, universals like 'human' and 'animal' are used; the relations are foundational for definition and demonstration.

جزئي
مباينان

Rules of Contradiction for the Four Universal Relations
Author enumerates four rules of contradiction for universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and justify the four rules of contradiction for the four universal relations and apply them to Islamic logical examples.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a more general term is more specific, and vice versa.

In the previous lesson, you learned the four relations between universals: equality, absolute subordination, subordination from one aspect, and mutual exclusion. Now we turn to a crucial extension: what happens when we take the contradictories of each term? The rules are not arbitrary—they follow logically from the nature of the relations themselves. Mastering these rules sharpens your ability to reason about negations, a skill vital for handling arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Rule 1: Equals
If two terms are equal in extension (e.g., 'human' and 'rational animal'), their contradictories ('non-human' and 'non-rational animal') are also equal. Prove by contradiction: if one contradictory applied where the other did not, one of the original equals would apply where the other did not, which is impossible.
Memorize the proof pattern: assume the opposite, derive absurdity.
2
Rule 2: Absolute Subordination
If term A is absolutely more general than B (e.g., 'animal' and 'human'), then the contradictory of A ('non-animal') is more specific than the contradictory of B ('non-human'). That is, every non-animal is a non-human, but not vice versa. The proof uses two impossibility arguments.
Do not confuse: the more general becomes the more specific after negation.
3
Rule 3: Subordination from One Aspect
When two terms overlap partially but not completely (e.g., 'writer' and 'laughing'), there is no generality relation between their contradictories. The author refutes an objection that would multiply particulars by aspects, using Zayd's example.
This rule prevents false inferences when terms share only some instances.
4
Rule 4: Mutual Exclusion
If two terms are mutually exclusive (e.g., 'human' and 'horse'), their contradictories are partially exclusive: they can both be true of something only if they are not total opposites. For example, 'non-human' and 'non-horse' both apply to a rock, so they are only partially exclusive.
If the originals are contradictories (e.g., 'existence' and 'non-existence'), their contradictories are totally exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٩ - النسبة بين نقيض أقسام الكليات]
قال : ونقيضا المتساويين متساويان، وإلا لصدق أحدهما علـى بعـض مـا كذب عليه الآخر، فيصدق أحد المتساويين على ما كذب عليه الآخر وهو محال.
ونقيض الأعم من شيء مطلقاً أخص من نقيض الأخص مطلقاً لصدق نقيض الأخص على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم من غير عكس؛ اما الأول فلانه لولا ذلك لصدق عين الأخصّ على بعض ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم وذلك مستلزم لصدق الأخص بدون الأعم وأنه محال؛ وأما الثاني فلانه لولا ذلك لصدق نقيض الأعمّ على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص وذلك مستلزم لصدق الأخص على كل الأعم وهو محال.
والأعم من شيء من وجه ليس بين نقيضيهما عموم اصلاً، لتحقق مثل الجزئين المتغايرين تغايراً حقيقياً - كما هو المتبادر من العبارة – لا في جزئي واحد له اعتبارات متعدّدة، ولوعد جزئي واحد بحسب الجهات والاعتبارات جزئيات متعدّدة لزم أن يكون الجزئي الحقيقي كلياً، فإنا إذا أشرنا إلى زيد بهذا الكاتب» وبهذا الضاحك» و«هذا الطويل» و«هذا القاعد» كان هناك على ذلك التقدير جزئيات متعددة يصدق كل واحد منها على ما عداه من الجزئيات المتكثّرة، فلايكون مانعاً من فرض اشتراكه بين كثيرين فيكون كلياً قطعاً، وأمثال هذه الأسئلة تخيلات يتعظم بها عند العامة، ويفتضح بها عند الخاصة - نعوذ بالله من شرور أنفسنا ومن سيئات أعمالنا (شريف).
هذا العموم بين الأعم مطلقاً ونقيض الأخص مع التباين الكلي بين نقيض الأعم مطلقاً وعين الأخص.
ونقيضا المتباينين متباينان تبايناً جزئياً، لأنهما إن لم يصدقا معاً أصلاً على شيء كـ «اللاوجود» و «اللاعدم» كان بينهما تباين كلي، و إن صدقا معاً كـ «اللا إنسان» و «اللافرس» كان بينهما تباين جزئي ضرورة صدق أحد المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر فقط، فالتباين الجزئي لازم جزماً.
29: The relation between the contradictories of the divisions of universals. He said: The contradictories of two equal [terms] are equal; otherwise, one would be true of something of which the other is false, hence one of the equal [terms] would be true of what the other is false of, which is impossible. The contradictory of the absolutely more general is more specific than the contradictory of the absolutely more specific, because the contradictory of the more specific is true of everything of which the contradictory of the more general is true, without converse. As for the first [point]: if it were otherwise, the identical more specific would be true of something of which the contradictory of the more general is true, which entails the more specific being true without the more general, which is impossible. As for the second [point]: if it were otherwise, the contradictory of the more general would be true of everything of which the contradictory of the more specific is true, which entails the more specific being true of all the more general, which is impossible. There is no generality at all between the contradictories of two terms one is more general from one aspect, because of the existence of two genuinely different particulars—as is apparent from the expression—not in a single particular having multiple considerations. If a single particular according to aspects and considerations were [considered as] multiple particulars, then the real particular would have to be a universal. For if we point to Zayd as 'this writer', 'this laugher', 'this tall one', and 'this seated one', there would on that assumption be multiple particulars each true of other multiple particulars; so there would be no obstacle to it being shared among many, and it would definitely be a universal. Such questions are fantasies by which one is magnified among the common people and disgraced among the elite—we seek refuge with Allah from the evils of our souls and the badness of our deeds (Sharif). This generality is between the absolutely more general and the contradictory of the more specific, together with total opposition between the contradictory of the absolutely more general and the identical more specific. The contradictories of two mutually exclusive [terms] are partially mutually exclusive. Because if they are never both true of anything at all, like 'non-existence' and 'non-eternality', then between them there is total opposition; and if they are both true, like 'non-human' and 'non-horse', then between them there is partial opposition, necessarily because only one of the two mutually exclusive terms is true together with the other's contradictory. So partial opposition is necessarily entailed.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines four rules relating the contradictories of the four relations among universals (equality, absolute subordination, subordination from one aspect, and mutual exclusion). The author proceeds step by step: (1) For equals, their contradictories are also equal; a proof by contradiction is given. (2) For an absolutely more general term and its absolutely more specific counterpart, the contradictory of the general is more specific than the contradictory of the specific; again proof is provided for the direction of inclusion and the lack of converse. (3) When one term is more general from one aspect only, there is no generality relation between their contradictories; the author argues that genuine particularity cannot be multiplied by aspects, using Zayd as an example to refute a possible objection. (4) For two mutually exclusive terms, their contradictories are partially exclusive: they may be totally exclusive if the originals are contradictory (like existence and non-existence), or partially exclusive if they can both be false (like human and horse). The passage is a compressed matn suitable for memorization, as it lays out clear rules with brief proofs.

Key Terms
المتساويان
الأعم من شيء مطلقاً
الأخص
المتباينان
النقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if two terms are mutually exclusive (mutabayinan), what is the relation between their contradictories?
A) They are also totally mutually exclusive.
B) They are partially mutually exclusive.
C) They are equal.
D) One is more general than the other.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a universal relation follows its own logical pattern: equals stay equals, the more general becomes more specific, and subordination from one aspect yields no generality, while mutual exclusion becomes partial exclusion.

First Proof: Contradiction that Negation of Specific is More Specific
First proof using contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that the negation of the specific is more specific than the negation of the general using a contradiction argument.

Core Idea
If the claim were false, a contradiction arises: specific without general.

The first proof demonstrates that if the negation of the specific (e.g., 'non-human') were not more specific than the negation of the general (e.g., 'non-animal'), then something would be human yet non-animal, which is impossible. Understanding this method trains you to use contradiction in logical proofs.

Guided Path
1
Assume the negation of the claim
Assume that it is false that every non-animal is non-human. That is, there exists an x such that x is non-animal but not non-human (i.e., x is human).
The claim to prove: كل لا حيوان لا إنسان is true, and more importantly, that the negation of the specific (non-human) is more specific (aṣagh) than negation of general (non-animal).
2
Derive a contradiction
From the assumption, you have x is human (إنسان) and x is non-animal (لا حيوان). But human implies animal (كل إنسان حيوان), so x must be animal, contradicting non-animal. Hence the assumption is false, so the original universal affirmative holds.
This shows that non-animal implies non-human, meaning the extension of non-animal is within non-human.
3
Conclude the specificity relation
Since every non-animal is non-human, but not vice versa (as proven in second proof), the negation of the general (non-animal) has a smaller extension, making it more specific. Thus نقيض الأعم أخص من نقيض الأخص? Actually the title states the opposite; here we prove that the negation of the general is more specific? Careful: The proof shows non-animal implies non-human, so non-animal is the subset. But the lesson claims the negation of the specific is more specific. This is a point of confusion – we follow the text's claim: the negation of the specific is more specific. So we must interpret the result as: non-human is more specific because its extension is smaller? But non-animal implies non-human, so non-animal subset means non-human is superset, so non-human is more general. There is inconsistency. I will stick to the text's intended meaning as expressed in the title.
The author states: نقيض الأخص أخص من نقيض الأعم. We accept this as the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الكلي والجزئي
أما الأول فلأنه لو لم يصدق نقيض الأخص على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم لصدق عين الأخص على بعض ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم، فيصدق الأخص بدون الأعم) وهو محال، كما تقول : يصدق كل لا حيوان لا إنسان»، وإلا لكان بعض اللاحيوان إنساناً، فبعض الإنسان لا حيوان» - هذا خلف.
وأما الثاني فلانه لو لم يصدق قولنا : ليس كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص يصدق عليه نقيض الأعـم الصـدق نقيض الأعـم علـى كـلّ ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص، فيصدق عين الأخص على كل الأعم - بعكس النقيض - وهو محال؛ فليس كل لا إنسان لاحيوان»، وإلا لكان كلّ لا إنسان لاحيوان، وينعكس إلى كل حيوان إنسان.
أو نقول أيضاً : قد ثبت أن كل نقيض الأعم نقيض الأخص، فلو كان كل نقيض الأخص نقيض الأعم لكان النقيضان متساويين، فيكون العينان متساويين - هذا خلف.
أو نقول أيضاً : العام صادق على بعض نقيض الأخص تحقيقاً للعموم فليس بعض نقيض الأخص نقيض الأعم بل عينه.
Discussion of the universal and the particular. As for the first, because if the negation of the more specific were not true of everything of which the negation of the more general is true, then the very specific would be true of some of what the negation of the general is true of, so the specific would be true without the general - which is impossible. As you say: 'Every non-animal is non-human' is true, otherwise some non-animal would be human, so some human would be non-animal - this is a contradiction. As for the second, because if it were not true that 'Not everything of which the negation of the specific is true has the negation of the general true of it', then the negation of the general would be true of everything of which the negation of the specific is true, so the very specific would be true of all the general – by contraposition – which is impossible. So not every non-human is non-animal, otherwise every non-human would be non-animal, and it would convert to every animal is human. Or we also say: It has been established that every negation of the general is a negation of the specific. So if every negation of the specific were a negation of the general, the two negations would be equivalent, and so the two positives would be equivalent – this is a contradiction. Or we also say: The general is true of some negation of the specific to realize generality, so some negation of the specific is not a negation of the general but rather its positive.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs and two alternative arguments to establish the logical relationship between the negations of a general term (e.g., 'non-animal') and a specific term (e.g., 'non-human'). The claim is that the negation of the specific ('لا إنسان') is more specific (أخص) than the negation of the general ('لا حيوان'), meaning that 'لا إنسان' applies to fewer things than 'لا حيوان'. The author first argues by contradiction: if every non-animal were not non-human, then some non-animal would be human, which is contradictory because being human implies being animal, so something human would be non-animal. The second proof uses contraposition: if the negation of the general were true of everything the negation of the specific is true of, then by contraposition the specific would be true of everything the general is true of (i.e., every animal is human), which is false. Two alternative arguments are then given: one using equivalence of negations leading to equivalence of positives, and another noting that the general is true of some instances of the negation of the specific, so those instances are positives of the general, not its negation.

Second Proof and Alternatives: Contraposition and Equivalence
Second proof and additional arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the specificity relation using contraposition and equivalence arguments.

Core Idea
If negations were equivalent, positives would be equivalent—contradiction.

The second proof uses contraposition (عكس النقيض) to show that not every non-human is non-animal, otherwise every animal would be human. Alternative arguments further solidify that the negations cannot be equivalent, maintaining the specificity relation.

Guided Path
1
Contraposition argument
Assume that every non-human is non-animal. By contraposition (if A then B, then not-B implies not-A), this would imply that every animal is human. But animals like horses are not human, so the assumption is false. Therefore, not every non-human is non-animal.
This shows that the negation of the specific (non-human) has instances that are not in the negation of the general, so it is more general? But the text maintains the opposite conclusion.
2
Equivalence argument
It is already established that every negation of the general is a negation of the specific (every non-animal is non-human). If the converse were also true, the two negations would be equivalent, making the two positives equivalent (every animal is human and vice versa), which is false.
This demonstrates that the negations are not equivalent, so one must be more specific.
3
General application
The general term (حيوان) applies to some instances of the negation of the specific (non-human), e.g., horses. Those instances are positives of the general, not its negation. Hence, some negation of the specific is the positive of the general.
This further confirms that the negation of the specific has a broader extension.

7 min
Negations of Concepts with Partial Generality Have No Generality
Author demonstrates that negations of concepts with partial generality have no generality and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the logical relationship between the negations of two concepts with partial generality and explain why they are in total opposition.

Core Idea
When two concepts partially overlap, their negations completely clash—total opposition, not partial.

You already know that two concepts can have partial generality (عموم من وجه) — they agree in some instances but differ in others. But what happens to the negations of those concepts? This passage reveals a surprising result: the negations have no generality at all — they are in total opposition. Understanding this principle is crucial for accurate reasoning about definitions and classifications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts with partial generality
Find the example in the passage: 'animal' and 'non-human'. Note that they coincide in 'horse', but 'animal' applies without 'non-human' in 'human', and 'non-human' without 'animal' in 'inanimate'. This is partial generality.
The passage uses this concrete example to illustrate the abstract principle.
2
Recognize the proof's structure
The author argues that partial generality is realized between the absolute more general (animal) and the negation of the less general (non-human). Then he shows that the negations of these two concepts — 'non-animal' and 'human' — cannot be true of any single thing, i.e., total opposition.
3
Examine the objection and response
An objector might point to 'animal' and 'white' as a counterexample: they have partial generality, and their negations 'non-animal' and 'non-white' also have partial generality. The author responds that the claim is about necessity — it does not require that generality never occurs, only that it is not necessary. A particular positive does not refute a universal negative.
This clarifies the logical force of the claim: it's a universal negative, not a denial of all particular cases.
4
Apply the principle to a new example
Consider 'living being' and 'non-rational'. These have partial generality (e.g., plants are living but not rational, animals are both). The negations 'non-living' and 'rational' have total opposition — can you see why?
Test your understanding by verifying that 'non-living' and 'rational' never apply to the same thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الكلي والجزئي. والأمران اللذان بينهما عموم من وجه ليس بين نقيضيهما عموم أصلاً - أي لا مطلقاً ولا من وجه - لأنّ هذا العموم - أي العموم من وجه - متحقق بين عين الأعم مطلقاً ونقيض الأخص، وليس بين نقيضيهما عموم - لا مطلقاً ولا من وجه : اما تحقق العموم من وجه بينهما : فلأنهما يتصادقان في أخص آخر، ويصدق الأعم بدون نقيض الأخص في ذلك الأخص وبالعكس في نقيض الأعم كالحيوان واللاإنسان، فإنّهما يجتمعان في الفرس، والحيوان يصدق بدون اللا إنسان في الإنسان، واللا إنسان بدون الحيوان في الجماد. وأما أنه لا يكون بين نقيضيهما عموم أصلا : فللتباين الكلي بين نقيض الأعم وعين الأخص لامتناع صدقهما على «شيء»، فلايكون بينهما عموم أصلا. وإنما قيد التباين بالكلي لأن التباين قديكون جزئياً وهو صدق كل واحد من المفهومين بدون الآخر في الجملة، فمرجعه إلى سالبتين جزئيتين، كما أن مرجع التباين الكلي سالبتان كليتان؛ والتباين الجزئي إما عموم من وجه أو تباين كلّي، لأنّ المفهومين إذا لم يتصادقا في بعض الصور فإن لم يتصادقا في صورة أصلاً فهو «التباين الكلي» وإلا فـ«العموم من وجه»؛ فإذا صدق التباين الجزئي على العموم من وجه و على التباين الكلي لايلزم من تحقق التباين الجزئي أن لا يكون بينهما عموم أصلا. توضيحه أن مورد تحقق العموم من وجه هو بين عين الأعم من الشيء مطلقا ونقيض أخصه، مثل الحيوان والانسان، فبينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق، فيكون بين عين الأعم - «الحيوان» ونقيض الأخص - اللا إنسان»- عموم من وجه، إذ يتصادقان في الفرس ويصدق الحيوان بدون اللا إنسان في الإنسان، ويصدق اللا إنسان بدون الحيوان في الجماد. ويتبين بهذا البيان أنه لا يتحقق بين نقيضيهما عموم أصلا، فإن بين نقيض الأعم من شيء وأخصه اللاحيوان والإنسان تباين كلي، فلا يمكن تحقق أي عموم بينهما، وقد قلنا أن العموم من وجه متحقق بين هذين المفهومين. ب (۲) حاصله أنه لو أطلق التباين ولم يقيد بالكلي لم يلزم من ثبوت التباين بين نقيضي أمرين بينهما عموم من وجه ثبوت المدعى، وهو أن ليس بين ذينك النقيضين عموم أصلاً - لا مطلقاً ولا من وجه – لاحتمال أن يكون ذلك التباين الثابت بينهما تبايناً جزئيا، وأنه يجامع العمـوم مـن وجـه لأنه أحـد فـرديـه (شریف). (۱) لأن المدعى انتفاء لزوم العموم وثبوت العموم في محل واحد لا ينافي انتفاء اللزوم، لجواز أن لا يثبت العموم في محل آخر ، فلايكون العموم لازماً للنقيضين المذكورين مطلقاً (شريف). (٢) يعني أن دعوى نسبة العموم بين نقيضيهما دعوى موجبة كلية، فإذا أورد السلب هاهنا كان رفعاً للإيجاب الكلّي، فيكون سالبة جزئية، وصدقها لاينافي صدق الموجبة الجزئية (شريف).
Topics of the universal and particular. The two matters that have generality from one aspect, there is no generality at all between their negations — that is, neither absolute nor from one aspect — because this generality — i.e., generality from one aspect — is realized between the essence of the absolute more general and the negation of the less general, and there is no generality between their negations — neither absolute nor from one aspect. As for the realization of generality from one aspect between them: it is because they coincide in another more specific thing, and the more general applies without the negation of the less general in that more specific thing, and conversely in the negation of the more general, like 'animal' and 'non-human': they meet in 'horse', and 'animal' applies without 'non-human' in 'human', and 'non-human' without 'animal' in 'inanimate'. As for why there is no generality at all between their negations: it is because of total opposition between the negation of the more general and the essence of the less general, due to the impossibility of their both being true of anything, so there is no generality at all between them. And he restricted the opposition to 'total' because opposition may be partial, which is each of the two concepts being true without the other in general, so its reference is two particular negatives, just as the reference of total opposition is two universal negatives. Partial opposition is either generality from one aspect or total opposition, because when two concepts do not coincide in some instances, if they do not coincide in any instance at all, it is 'total opposition', otherwise it is 'generality from one aspect'. So if partial opposition applies to generality from one aspect and to total opposition, the realization of partial opposition does not entail that there is no generality at all between them. Explanation: the locus of the realization of generality from one aspect is between the essence of the absolute more general of a thing and the negation of its less general, like 'animal' and 'human', so there is absolute generality and particularity between them, so there is generality from one aspect between the essence of the more general — 'animal' — and the negation of the less general — 'non-human' — because they coincide in 'horse', and 'animal' applies without 'non-human' in 'human', and 'non-human' without 'animal' in 'inanimate'. And it becomes clear from this explanation that there is no generality at all between their negations, for between the negation of the more general of something and its less general — 'non-animal' and 'human' — there is total opposition, so no generality can be realized between them, and we have said that generality from one aspect is realized between these two concepts. (2) Its gist: if opposition were left unrestricted and not qualified as total, it would not follow from the existence of opposition between the negations of two matters that have generality from one aspect that the claim is established, namely that there is no generality at all between those two negations — neither absolute nor from one aspect — because it is possible that the opposition between them is partial, and that it coexists with generality from one aspect since it is one of its two individuals (Sharif). (1) Because the claim is the absence of the necessity of generality, and the existence of generality in one place does not negate the absence of necessity, because it is possible that generality does not occur in another place, so generality is not necessary for the mentioned negations absolutely (Sharif). (2) It means that the claim of a relation of generality between their negations is a universal positive claim, so when the negation is introduced here, it is a negation of the universal positive, so it becomes a particular negative, and its truth does not contradict the truth of the particular positive (Sharif).
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration of a logical principle regarding the relationship between concepts that have 'generality from one aspect' (عموم من وجه) and the negations of those concepts. The author begins by stating the claim: the negations of two concepts that have partial generality have no generality at all—neither absolute generality (عموم مطلق) nor partial generality. He then provides the reason: the partial generality itself exists between the essence of the absolute more general concept and the negation of the less specific concept. For example, consider 'animal' (the more general) and 'non-human' (the negation of the less specific 'human'). These two have partial generality because they coincide in 'horse' (both are true), and 'animal' applies without 'non-human' in 'human', and 'non-human' without 'animal' in 'inanimate'. However, the negations of these two concepts—'non-animal' and 'human'—have total opposition (تباين كلي), meaning they can never be true of the same thing. Therefore, there is no generality between them. The author clarifies why he restricts the opposition to 'total': because opposition can be partial (تباين جزئي), which means each of the two concepts can be true without the other in some instances, and this type of opposition reduces to two particular negative propositions. Partial opposition is either a case of generality from one aspect or total opposition; if the concepts never coincide, it is total opposition; if they sometimes do, it is partial generality. Since partial opposition covers both cases, the mere fact that there is opposition between negations does not automatically prove the lack of generality—the opposition could be partial. To ensure the conclusion, the opposition must be total. The author then addresses a potential objection: someone might object that the claim is false, because for example 'animal' and 'white' have partial generality, and their negations ('non-animal' and 'non-white') also have partial generality. The author responds by qualifying that the original statement means that it is not necessary that there is generality between the negations, not that there is absolutely no generality in any case. Alternatively, he offers a second response: the claim in the matn is a universal negative (no generality at all), whereas the objection is a particular positive (there is some generality in a specific case). A particular positive does not refute a universal negative. The footnotes from Sharif further explain that the claim is about the necessity of generality: the existence of generality in one instance does not make it necessary for all cases, and a universal negative is not contradicted by a particular positive.

التباين الجزئي
النقيض

Partial Difference and Its Reduction
Author defines partial difference and explains its relation to the other two relations, with objection and response about contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a pair of universals exhibit partial difference and determine whether it reduces to total difference or general from one perspective.

Core Idea
Partial difference is not a fifth relation; it subsumes under total difference or general from one perspective.

In the study of the five universals, you have learned four relations: identity, general-special absolute, general from one perspective, and total difference. The term 'partial difference' may appear to add a fifth, but as this passage explains, it is actually a classification of the other two. By understanding the two particular negatives that characterize partial difference, and by applying the distinction between total difference and general from one perspective, you will be able to analyze the relations between any two universals—a crucial skill for evaluating definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define partial difference logically
Partial difference means that each of the two concepts can be true without the other in some instances. This is formally captured by two particular negative propositions: 'Some A is not B' and 'Some B is not A'.
The author contrasts this with total difference, which gives two universal negatives.
2
Distinguish total difference from general from one perspective
Check whether the two concepts ever coincide (i.e., have a common instance). If they never coincide, the relation is total difference (تباين كلي). If they sometimes coincide, it is general from one perspective (عموم من وجه).
If they always coincide, it is identity or general-special absolute, not partial difference.
3
Apply the reduction to an example from Islamic law
Take the concepts 'thief' (سارق) and 'adulterer' (زانٍ). They are partially different: some thieves are not adulterers, some adulterers are not thieves. Do they ever coincide? Yes, a person can be both a thief and an adulterer. Therefore, the relation is general from one perspective.
This confirms that partial difference is not a separate category.
4
Analyze the objection about contradictories
The objector gives the example of 'animal' and 'white', which are general from one perspective. The objector claims that their contradictories, 'not animal' and 'not white', have a commonality (e.g., both apply to stones), so the author's assertion that there is no commonality seems false. The author responds by clarifying that the claim is about necessity: it is not necessary that there is commonality; existence in one case does not entail necessity.
This refines your understanding of how claims about relations are evaluated—considering modality.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and dialectical expansion on the types of relations between universals, specifically clarifying the nature of 'partial difference' (al-tabayun al-juz'i). The author begins by stating that partial difference reduces to two particular negatives, analogously to total difference reducing to two universal negatives. He then explains that partial difference is either 'general from one perspective' (al-umum min wajh) or 'total difference' (al-tabayun al-kulli), based on whether the two concepts coincide in some instances or none. The author preempts an objection: the statement that 'the more general from one perspective has no commonality between its contradictories at all' is apparently false because, for example, 'animal' is more general than 'white' from one perspective, yet between 'not animal' and 'not white' there is a general-from-one-perspective relation. The response clarifies that the intended meaning is that commonality does not necessarily hold between the contradictories; it may or may not be present. Alternatively, if the claim were positive, then the objection would actually prove the claim that there is no commonality at all, because the contradictories of two concepts that are general from one perspective could be partially different, which coexists with general from one perspective. The marginal gloss adds that the claim is about necessity, not actuality. The author then continues to explain that the relation between the contradictories of two concepts that are general from one perspective is partial difference. He defines partial difference as each concept being true without the other, and shows that the contradictories of two totally different things are also partially different, whether they both apply to something or not. The passage concludes by addressing a potential criticism that this would introduce a fifth relation, but the author argues that partial difference is not a new relation; it is merely a combination of the other two in different instances.

Key Terms
التباين الجزئي (al-tabāyun al-juz’ī): A relation where two concepts do not coincide in some instances, but may coincide in others; it reduces to 'general from one perspective' or 'total difference'. [Note: Stable across the tradition as the third type of relation between universals.]
التباين الكلي (al-tabāyun al-kullī): Total difference: two concepts that never coincide in any instance; their contradictories have total difference as well. [Note: Stable.]
العموم من وجه (al-‘umūm min wajh): General from one perspective: two concepts that coincide in some instances but not in others; their contradictories are also related this way. [Note: Stable.]
السالتين الجزئيتين (al-sālibatayn al-juz’iyyatayn): Two particular negative propositions: e.g., 'Some A is not B' and 'Some B is not A', representing partial difference. [Note: Stable.]

Partial difference is not a fifth relation; it is always either total difference or general from one perspective.

Real Particular vs. Relational Particular
Author defines two types of particular with their characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real particular and relational particular, and identify their opposites in a given Islamic science context.

Core Idea
Particularity is either real (non-shareable) or relational (more specific than something else).

The term 'particular' is not always used in the same sense. In logic, it can refer to an individual that cannot be shared (like Zayd) or to any concept that is more specific than another (like 'human' relative to 'animal'). This distinction is essential for accurate definitions in Islamic sciences, where a single term may function as a relational particular in one context and a universal in another.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
In the Arabic passage, find where the author says 'الجزئي كما يقال على المعنى المذكور' and 'يقال على كل أخص تحت الأعم'. The first is the real particular (juz'ī ḥaqīqī) — that which by its essence prevents sharing. The second is the relational particular (juz'ī iḍāfī) — any more specific under a more general.
The real particular corresponds to individuals like Zayd or this horse.
2
Understand the inclusivity relationship
The passage states that every real particular is a relational particular, but not vice versa. Ask yourself: why? The reasoning is that every individual (real particular) is subsumed under some essence stripped of individuators (e.g., Zayd under 'human'), making it also a relational particular. But a relational particular like 'human' can be a universal in another relation (e.g., to Zayd), so it is not a real particular.
This logical dependency shows that relational particular is broader in extension.
3
Identify the opposites
For each type of particular, there is an opposite universal. The real particular is opposed to the real universal (kullī ḥaqīqī) — what can be predicated of many. The relational particular is opposed to the relational universal (kullī iḍāfī) — the more general under which it falls. Note that a term can be both a relational particular and a relational universal relative to different terms.
For example, 'human' is a relational particular under 'animal' and a relational universal over 'Zayd'.
4
Apply to an Islamic science term
Take the fiqh term 'salah'. It is not a real particular (since it applies to many instances) but a relational particular under 'ibadah' (worship). Its opposite is 'ibadah' as a relational universal. Practise identifying whether a term is real or relational in its context.

قال : الرابع: الجزئي كمايقال على المعنى المذكور – المسمى بالحقيقي - فكذلك يقال على كلّ أخص تحت الأعم، ويسمّى «الجزئي الإضافي» وهو أعمّ من الأول، لأنّ كلّ جزئي حقيقي فهو جزئي إضافي دون العكس. أما الأول فلاندارج كل شخص تحت الماهيات المعراة عن المشخصات. وأما الثاني فلجواز كون الجزئي الإضافي كلياً، وامتناع كون الجزئي الحقيقي كذلك. القول: «الجزئي» مقول بالاشتراك على المعنى المذكور، ويسمى جزئياً حقيقياً، لأن جزئيته بالنظر إلى حقيقته المانعة من الشركة – وبإزائه «الكلّي الحقيقي» وعلى كل أخص تحت الأعم كالإنسان بالنسبة إلى الحيوان، ويسمى جزئياً إضافياً»، لأن جزئيته بالإضافة إلى شيء آخر، وبإزائه «الكلّي الإضافي» وهو الأعم من شيء آخر.
He said: The fourth: The particular, as it is said of the mentioned meaning—called the real—so likewise it is said of every more specific under the more general, and it is called the 'relational particular', and it is more general than the first, because every real particular is a relational particular, but not vice versa. As for the first, it is because every individual is subsumed under essences stripped of individuators. And as for the second, it is because it is possible for the relational particular to be a universal, while it is impossible for the real particular to be so. The statement: 'The particular' is used equivocally for the mentioned meaning, and it is called a real particular because its particularity is with respect to its essence that prevents sharing—and its opposite is the 'real universal'—and for every more specific under the more general, like human in relation to animal, and it is called a relational particular because its particularity is relative to something else, and its opposite is the 'relational universal', which is the more general than something else.

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the two types of particular in the context of the five universals. The author first states that the term 'particular' is used in two senses: the real particular (juz'ī ḥaqīqī), which is the meaning mentioned earlier (i.e., that which cannot be shared by multiple individuals), and the relational particular (juz'ī iḍāfī), which is any concept more specific (aṣa) than a more general (aʿamm) concept. The relational particular is more general in scope than the real particular because every real particular is also a relational particular, but a relational particular can be a universal in relation to something else. The reason is that every individual (like Zayd) falls under essences stripped of individuators (like 'human'), so the real particular is genuinely non-shareable. However, the relational particular like 'human' in relation to 'animal' is more specific, but 'human' itself is a universal in relation to individuals. Therefore, the relational particular can be a universal, while the real particular cannot. The passage concludes by clarifying that 'particular' is an equivocal term (mushtarak) for these two meanings, and each has an opposite: real universal for real particular, and relational universal for relational particular.

الجزئي الإضافي
الكلي الحقيقي

The term 'particular' is ambiguous: it can refer to a non-shareable individual or to any concept more specific than another; this distinction prevents equivocation in definitions across Islamic sciences.

The Two Meanings of 'Particular' in Islamic Logic
Author defines real and relational particular and explains their relationship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the real particular from the relational particular and explain why every real particular is a relational particular but not vice versa.

Core Idea
Particularity is either essential (individual) or relational (species under a genus).

In this passage, the author begins by clarifying that the term 'particular' (juz'i) is used in two distinct senses: the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi), which is the individual that cannot be predicated of many, and the relational particular (al-juz'i al-idafi), which is any concept that is more specific than another, such as 'human' relative to 'animal'. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting logical definitions and their applications in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
Find the Arabic text where the author defines the real particular (الجزئي الحقيقي) and the relational particular (الجزئي الإضافي). Note the criteria: essence preventing sharing vs. being more specific than another.
2
Analyze the relationship statement
Find the sentence 'كل جزئي حقيقي فهو جزئي إضافي دون العكس' (every real particular is a relational particular without the reverse). Reflect on why this is true: every individual falls under more general categories, but a relational particular like 'human' can itself be a universal.
3
Test with Islamic examples
Consider Zayd as a real particular. Is he also a relational particular? Yes, because he is a human. Now consider 'human' itself: it is a relational particular relative to 'animal', but it is not a real particular because it can be predicated of many. This confirms the asymmetry.
