The Four Ranks of Relative Species and a Refinement
Tahzeeb enumerates four ranks of relative species, followed by a critique and refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four ranks of relative species and explain al-Sharif's refined definition that removes the problematic condition.

Core Idea
The hierarchy of species exposes the flaw in the initial definition, leading to a more precise formulation.

After defining relative species with the condition of primary predication, the passage enumerates its four hierarchical ranks. The highest, lowest, intermediate, and unique species are distinguished. Then a critical note argues that the condition of primary predication is too restrictive, because it would exclude a species like 'man' from being predicated of remote genera such as 'substance'. This forces a refinement: dropping the condition and redefining relative species in a way that still excludes subordinate genera.

Guided Path
1
List the four ranks
Read the passage carefully and identify the four ranks: highest species (النوع العالي), lowest species (النوع السافل), intermediate species (النوع المتوسط), and unique species (النوع المفرد).
Examples are given: body is highest, man is lowest, animal is intermediate, intellect is unique (if substance is its genus).
2
Understand the problem with the initial condition
The condition of primary predication, while excluding subordinate genera, also excludes species from being predicated of remote genera. For instance, 'man' would not be a species of 'body' or 'substance' because the predication is not primary.
This contradicts the accepted fact that man is a species of all genera above it (hence called 'species of species').
3
Analyze the correlativity argument
The critic notes that species and genus are correlatives (مضايفان). If primary predication is required for species, it must also be required for genus; otherwise, they would not be mutual. This would make remote genera no longer genera of the lower species.
This strengthens the case for dropping the condition.
4
Study al-Sharif's alternative definition
The refinement defines relative species simply as a universal that is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' and upon which and upon others the genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' — without the condition of primary predication.
The removal of the condition avoids the contradictions while still excluding subordinate genera through other means (perhaps by the requirement that the genus be predicated of it and others directly).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوّلي ، فقوله : «قولاً أولياً» احتراز عن الصنف، فإنّه كلّي يقال عليه وعلى غيره الجنس في جواب ما هو، حتى إذا سئل عن التركي والفرس بـ«ماهما»؟ كان الجواب : «الحيوان لكن قول الجنس على الصنف ليس بأولي، بل بواسطة حمل النوع عليه، فاعتبار الأولية في القول يخرج الصنف عن الحد، لأنه يسمّى نوعاً إضافياً.
٣٢- مراتب النوع الإضافي]
قال: ومراتبه أربع لأنه أما أعمّ الأنواع وهو «النوع العالي» كالجسم. أو أخصها وهو النوع السافل كالإنسان ويسمى «نوع الأنواع». أواعم من السافل وأخص من العالي وهو «النوع المتوسط» كالحيوان والجسم النامي. أو مباين للكل وهو النوع المفرد كالعقل - إن قلنا أن الجوهر جنس له.
(1) هذا القيد وإن أخرج الصنف عن الحد أخرج النوع عنه أيضاً بالقياس إلى الأجناس البعيدة، فيلزم أن لا يكون الإنسان» نوعاً للجسم النامي ، ولا للجسم ولا للجوهر ، مع أنه يسمّى «نوع الأنواع» لكونه نوعاً لكل واحد من الأنواع التي فوقه. وأيضاً النوع لما كان مضايفاً للجنس، فإذا اعتبر في النوع «القول الأولي» فلابد من اعتباره في الجنس أيضاً، وإلا لم يكن مضايفاله، فيلزم أن لا تكون الأجناس البعيدة أجناساً للماهية التي هي بعيدة بالقياس إليها، فالأولى أن يترك قيد «الأولية» ويُخرج الصنف بقيد آخر ويقال : «النوع الإضافي كلّي مقول في جواب «ماهو ؟» يقال عليه وعلى غيره الجنس في جواب ماهو» (شریف).
So his saying: 'a primary predication' is a precaution against the subordinate genus, for it is a universal that is predicated of itself and of others by the genus in answer to 'what is it?' such that when asked about the Turk and the horse 'what are they?', the answer is 'animal'. But the predication of the genus upon the subordinate genus is not primary; rather, it is through the mediation of the species being predicated of it. So considering primary in predication excludes the subordinate genus from the definition, because it is called a relative species.
[32- Ranks of relative species]
He said: and its ranks are four: for it is either the most general of species, which is the 'highest species' like body; or the most specific, which is the 'lowest species' like man, and it is called 'species of species'; or more general than the lowest and more specific than the highest, which is the 'intermediate species' like animal and the growing body; or separate from all, which is the 'unique species' like intellect – if we say that substance is a genus for it.
(1) This condition, although it excludes the subordinate genus from the definition, also excludes the species from it with respect to the remote genera. Consequently, man would not be a species for the growing body, nor for body, nor for substance, while he is called 'species of species' because he is a species for each of the species above him. Also, since species is correlative to genus, if the 'primary predication' is considered in species, it must also be considered in genus; otherwise they would not be correlatives. Consequently, remote genera would not be genera for the essence that is remote with respect to them. (Sharif).

Definitions of Highest Genus and Lowest Species
Author defines relational conditions for jins al-ajnas and naw' al-anwa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify jins al-ajnas and naw' al-anwa' based on their positions in the genus-species hierarchy and explain the relational logic behind their names.

Core Idea
Highest genus is above all genera; lowest species is under all species.

The author clarifies why the highest genus is called 'genus of genera' and the lowest species 'species of species'. The key is that genus-ness is measured by what is below, and species-ness by what is above. Understanding this relational definition is essential for navigating the hierarchy of universals.

Guided Path
1
Understand the relational nature of genus and species
The author states: 'جنسية الشيء إنما هي بالقياس إلى ماتحته' (genus-ness of a thing is only relative to what is beneath it). A term is a genus because it applies to more specific types below it, not because of what is above it.
2
Define jins al-ajnas
A term is jins al-ajnas (genus of genera) when it is above all other genera, i.e., it has no genus above it. The author says: 'فهو إنما يكون جنس الأجناس إذا كان فوق جميع الأجناس'.
This is the highest point in the Porphyrian tree.
3
Define naw' al-anwa'
A term is naw' al-anwa' (species of species) when it is under all other species, i.e., it has no species beneath it. The author says: 'فهو إنما يكون نوع الأنواع إذا كان تحت جميع الأنواع'.
This is the lowest point in the tree, directly above individuals.
4
Apply the definitions to a real example
Consider the term 'human' (insan). Below it are only individuals, not lower species, so it is naw' al-anwa'. Above it is genus 'animal' (hayawan). This shows the relational nature.
The author's own example comes later; here we use a familiar Islamic science example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أن العالي في مراتب الأجناس يسمّى «جنس الأجناس» لا السافل، والسافل في مراتب الأنواع يسمّى «نوع الأنواع»- لا العالي - وذلك لأنّ جنسية الشيء إنما هي بالقياس إلى ماتحته، فهو إنما يكون جنس الأجناس إذا كان فوق جميع الأجناس، ونوعية الشيء إنما تكون بالقياس إلى مافوقه، فهو إنما يكون نوع الأنواع إذا كان تحت جميع الأنواع. والجنس المفرد ممثل بالعقل على تقدير أن لا يكون الجوهر جنساً له، فإنّه ليس أعم من جنس - إذ ليس تحته إلا العقول العشرة، وهي أنواع لا أجناس - ولا أخص إذ ليس فوقه إلا الجوهر، وقد فرض أنه ليس بجنس له.
(۱
لا يقال : أحد التمثيلين فاسد ؛ إما تمثيل النوع المفرد بالعقل على تقدير جنسية الجوهر؛ و إما تمثيل الجنس المفرد بالعقل على تقدير عرضية الجوهر، لأن العقل إن كان جنساً يكون تحته أنواع فلايكون نوعاً مفرداً، بل كان عالياً ، فلا يصح التمثيل الأول ، وإن لم يكن جنساً لم يصح التمثيل الثاني ضرورة أن ما لا يكون جنساً لا يكون جنساً مفرداً.
لأنا نقول : التمثيل الأول على تقدير أن العقول العشرة متفقة بالنوع.
(۱) قد عرفت أن تمثيل الأول مبني على اتفاق العقول العشرة في الحقيقة وكون الجوهر جنساً لها، والتمثيل الثاني موقوف على اختلافها في الحقيقة وكون الجوهر ليس جنساً لها، فيستحيل صحتهما معا. ف
والا
والجواب : أن المقصود من التمثيل هو التفهيم، فإن طابق الواقع فذاك، لم يضر ، إذ يكفيه مجرد الفرض - خصوصا فيما لم يوجد له مثال في الوجود ظاهراً (شريف).
والثاني على تقدير أنها مختلفة فيه والتمثيل يحصل بمجرد الفرض سواء طابق الواقع أو لم يطابقه.
Except that the highest in the ranks of genera is called 'genus of genera', not the lowest, and the lowest in the ranks of species is called 'species of species'—not the highest—and that is because the genus-ness of a thing is only relative to what is beneath it, so it is only genus of genera if it is above all genera, and the species-ness of a thing is only relative to what is above it, so it is only species of species if it is under all species. And the singular genus is represented by the intellect on the assumption that substance is not a genus for it, for it is not more general than a genus—since beneath it are only the ten intellects, which are species not genera—and not more specific since above it is only substance, and it is assumed that it is not a genus for it. (1) Do not say: One of the two representations is corrupt; either the representation of the singular species by the intellect on the assumption of substance being a genus for it, or the representation of the singular genus by the intellect on the assumption of substance being an accident for it, because if the intellect is a genus, it has species beneath it, so it is not a singular species but rather high, so the first representation is not sound, and if it is not a genus, the second representation is not sound necessarily, because that which is not a genus is not a singular genus. To this we say: The first representation is on the assumption that the ten intellects are agreed in species. (1) You have known that the first representation is based on agreement of the ten intellects in reality and substance being a genus for them, and the second representation depends on their difference in reality and substance not being a genus for them, so it is impossible for both to be sound. Then the answer: The purpose of the representation is making understood; if it matches reality, that is fine, if not, it does not harm, for mere assumption suffices—especially in what has no manifest example in existence (al-Sharif). And the second is on the assumption that they are different in it, and the representation is obtained by mere assumption whether it matches reality or not.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the highest genus (jins al-ajnas) and the lowest species (naw' al-anwa'), then presents and resolves an objection about the hypothetical examples. The author explains that a genus is called 'genus of genera' when it is above all other genera, meaning nothing is more general than it. Conversely, a species is called 'species of species' when it is under all other species, meaning nothing is more specific than it. Two singular examples are given using 'intellect' ('aql): the singular genus (jins mufrad) on the assumption that substance is not its genus, and the singular species (naw' mufrad) on the assumption that substance is its genus. An objector argues that both examples cannot be sound because they rely on contradictory assumptions about substance being a genus for intellect. The response clarifies that the examples serve only for illustration (tafhīm), not necessarily as real existents. Mere assumption suffices, especially when no actual example is manifest. Therefore, both representations can stand as hypotheticals despite their logical incompatibility as actualities. The commentary here reinforces that in logical definitions, thought experiments are valid for clarifying concepts.

Key Terms
جنس الأجناس
نوع الأنواع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the defining condition for something to be called 'naw' al-anwa' (species of species)?
A) It is the most general term.
B) It is under all species with no species beneath it.
C) It is the same as a singular genus.
D) It has both a genus above and species below.

Key Takeaway
Jins al-ajnas is defined by having no genus above it; naw' al-anwa' is defined by having no species beneath it; both are relational terms in the genus-species hierarchy.

?

Hypothetical Examples and the Objection's Resolution
Author defends hypothetical examples and resolves an objection about their logical consistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the hypothetical examples of jins mufrad and naw' mufrad using 'aql, and analyze the objection and defense regarding their logical compatibility.

Core Idea
Hypothetical examples are valid for teaching even if they are not actual.

After establishing the definitions, the author provides two examples using the intellect ('aql) to illustrate a singular genus (jins mufrad) and a singular species (naw' mufrad). An objector argues that the two examples are mutually contradictory. The author's response reveals an important principle: in logical education, mere assumptions suffice to convey concepts, especially when real examples are not manifest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two hypothetical examples
First example: jins mufrad (singular genus) represented by intellect on the assumption that substance (jawhar) is NOT its genus. Second example: naw' mufrad (singular species) represented by intellect on the assumption that substance IS its genus.
The difference in assumption about substance being a genus is key.
2
Understand the objector's critique
The objector says: one of the two examples must be corrupt because they require contradictory conditions. If intellect is a genus, it cannot be a singular species; if it is not a genus, it cannot be a singular genus. The two assumptions cannot both be true simultaneously.
The objector demands consistency in the examples.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author clarifies that the first example assumes the ten intellects are all of the same species (متفقة بالنوع), while the second assumes they differ in species. Since these are different assumptions, both representations can be used separately for illustration. The purpose is 'tafhīm' (making understood), not positing actual existents.
The author also adds that mere hypothesis suffices, especially when no clear real example exists.
4
Apply the principle to another field
In Usul al-Fiqh, jurists often use hypothetical scenarios (taqdir) to illustrate principles, such as assuming a certain condition exists to derive a ruling. This is accepted as long as it aids understanding.

Except that the highest in the ranks of genera is called 'genus of genera', not the lowest, and the lowest in the ranks of species is called 'species of species'—not the highest—and that is because the genus-ness of a thing is only relative to what is beneath it, so it is only genus of genera if it is above all genera, and the species-ness of a thing is only relative to what is above it, so it is only species of species if it is under all species. And the singular genus is represented by the intellect on the assumption that substance is not a genus for it, for it is not more general than a genus—since beneath it are only the ten intellects, which are species not genera—and not more specific since above it is only substance, and it is assumed that it is not a genus for it. (1) Do not say: One of the two representations is corrupt; either the representation of the singular species by the intellect on the assumption of substance being a genus for it, or the representation of the singular genus by the intellect on the assumption of substance being an accident for it, because if the intellect is a genus, it has species beneath it, so it is not a singular species but rather high, so the first representation is not sound, and if it is not a genus, the second representation is not sound necessarily, because that which is not a genus is not a singular genus. To this we say: The first representation is on the assumption that the ten intellects are agreed in species. (1) You have known that the first representation is based on agreement of the ten intellects in reality and substance being a genus for them, and the second representation depends on their difference in reality and substance not being a genus for them, so it is impossible for both to be sound. Then the answer: The purpose of the representation is making understood; if it matches reality, that is fine, if not, it does not harm, for mere assumption suffices—especially in what has no manifest example in existence (al-Sharif). And the second is on the assumption that they are different in it, and the representation is obtained by mere assumption whether it matches reality or not.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the highest genus (jins al-ajnas) and the lowest species (naw' al-anwa'), then presents and resolves an objection about the hypothetical examples. The author explains that a genus is called 'genus of genera' when it is above all other genera, meaning nothing is more general than it. Conversely, a species is called 'species of species' when it is under all other species, meaning nothing is more specific than it. Two singular examples are given using 'intellect' ('aql): the singular genus (jins mufrad) on the assumption that substance is not its genus, and the singular species (naw' mufrad) on the assumption that substance is its genus. An objector argues that both examples cannot be sound because they rely on contradictory assumptions about substance being a genus for intellect. The response clarifies that the examples serve only for illustration (tafhīm), not necessarily as real existents. Mere assumption suffices, especially when no actual example is manifest. Therefore, both representations can stand as hypotheticals despite their logical incompatibility as actualities. The commentary here reinforces that in logical definitions, thought experiments are valid for clarifying concepts.

Key Terms
الجنس المفرد
النوع المفرد
العقل
التقدير

B) He is illustrating that logic can handle contradictory scenarios.
C) The purpose is simply to make the concepts understood, and assumptions suffice.
D) He wants to show that the intellect is both a genus and a species simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Hypothetical examples in logic are valid as long as they serve to illustrate concepts, even if they are not actual; the goal is conceptual understanding, not empirical truth.

Relationship Between Real and Relative Species
Author clarifies the logical relationship between real and relative species

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute generality ('umum mutlaq) and generality from one aspect ('umum min wajh), and correctly identify the relationship between real and relative species.

Core Idea
Real and relative species overlap: each is more general than the other from different angles.

This passage clarifies how two key logical concepts—real species (naw' haqiqi) and relative species (naw' idafi)—relate to each other. Understanding this relationship is crucial for building correct definitions and avoiding a common mistake made by earlier logicians who assumed absolute generality. The author uses a clever dialectical strategy to refute that position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'species'
The passage distinguishes real species (simple realities that have no lower species) from relative species (intermediate species that are both genus and species). Note examples: 'simple realities' like 'human' as a species with no genera under it, and 'intermediate species' like 'animal' which is a species relative to 'living being' and a genus relative to 'human'.
These are classical logical terms; ensure you can define each.
2
Understand the mistaken view of the ancients
Earlier logicians thought that relative species is absolutely more general than real species. They likely reasoned that because relative species can be a genus, it covers more. Identify why this view seems plausible but fails.
The mistake is conflating 'being a genus' with 'always being more general'.
3
Analyze the author's refutation strategy
The author does not directly refute the specific claim; instead, he refutes a stronger claim—that the two are related by absolute generality in either direction. By proving this stronger claim false, the specific claim automatically falls. This is a powerful dialectical move: attack the more general to invalidate the specific.
This strategy appears in many Islamic logical debates.
4
Grasp the correct relationship: 'umum min wajh
Each type is more general from one aspect: real species is more general in that it includes all simple realities; relative species is more general in that it includes all intermediate species. They overlap on the lowest species (e.g., 'human' is both a real species and falls under relative species). This is not absolute generality but partial overlap.
Use the Arabic phrase 'عموم من وجه' as a technical term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والنوع الإضافي موجود بدون الحقيقي كالأنواع المتوسطة، والحقيقي موجود بدون الإضافي كالحقائق البسيطة، فليس بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق، بل كلّ منهما أعمّ من الآخر من وجه - لصدقهما على النوع السافل.
القول : لما نبه على أن للنوع معنيين أراد أن يبين النسبة بينهما،
(۱) حاصله أن المصنف أراد أن يبيّن أن النسبة بين المعنيين هي العموم من وجه، لكن لما كان القدماء توهموا أن الإضافي أعم مطلقاً من الحقيقي ردّ أولا قولهم في صورة دعوى أعم من قولهم، ثم بين أن النسبة بينهما هي العموم من وجه. فهاهنا ثلاثة أشياء : أحدها بيان أن النسبة بينهما هي العموم من وجه - وهذا هو المقصود الأصلي. وثانيها رد قولهم صريحاً، وذلك للاهتمام بهذا الردّ وللمبالغة فيه حتى لا يتوهم كون قولهم صحيحاً؛ ولو اكتفى ببيان أن النسبة هي العموم من وجه لكان يفهم من ذلك ردّ قولهم - ولكن ضمناً لا صريحاً.
وثالثها رد قولهم في صورة دعوى أعم من قولهم؛ وذلك لأنهم زعموا أن الإضافي أعم مطلقا؛ فردّ هذا القول هو أن يقال : «ليس الإضافي أعم مطلقاً، لوجود الحقيقي بدونه كما في الحقائق البسيطة»، والمصنف ردّ ماهو أعم من قولهم - وهو أن النسبة بينهما العموم أن النسبة بينهما العموم مطلقاً - فقال : «ليس بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق» وإذا بطل ما هو أعم من قولهم بطل قولهم، لأن الأعم
He said: And the relative species exists without the real species, like the intermediate species, and the real species exists without the relative species, like the simple realities. So there is not between them absolute generality and specificity, but rather each of them is more general than the other from one aspect—because they both apply to the lowest species. The commentary: When he drew attention to the fact that species has two meanings, he wanted to clarify the ratio between them. (1) The summary is that the author wanted to clarify that the ratio between the two meanings is generality from one aspect. But because the ancients imagined that the relative species is absolutely more general than the real species, he first refuted their statement in the form of a claim that is more general than their statement, then he clarified that the ratio between them is generality from one aspect. So here there are three things: One of them is the clarification that the ratio between them is generality from one aspect—and this is the intended purpose. The second is the explicit refutation of their statement, for the sake of emphasis and insistence on this refutation so that their statement is not imagined to be correct; and had he been content with clarifying that the ratio is generality from one aspect, the refutation of their statement would be understood from that—but implicitly, not explicitly. And the third is the refutation of their statement in the form of a claim that is more general than their statement; that is because they claimed that the relative species is absolutely more general. So the refutation of this statement is to say: 'The relative species is not absolutely more general, because the real species exists without it as in the simple realities.' And the author refuted what is more general than their statement—namely that the ratio between them is absolute generality—so he said: 'There is not between them absolute generality and specificity.' And if what is more general than their statement is invalidated, their statement is invalidated, because the more general...
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage clarifies the logical relationship between the two meanings of 'species' discussed in logic: real species (naw' haqiqi) and relative species (naw' idafi). The author first states that relative species can exist without real species, as in intermediate species that are both species and genera, and real species can exist without relative species, as in simple realities that are pure species with no genera beneath them. Consequently, they are not related by absolute generality-specificity ('umum wa khusus mutlaq); rather, each is more general than the other from one aspect ('umum min wajh) because both apply to the lowest species. The commentary expands on this, noting the author's triple purpose: (1) to show the relation is 'umum min wajh, (2) to explicitly refute the earlier mistaken view that relative species is absolutely more general, and (3) to refute an even stronger claim that the relation is absolute generality. By refuting the stronger claim, the weaker claim is automatically refuted. The logical move here is careful: the author attacks the claim that the two are related by absolute generality (which the ancients implicitly held), and since that is false, the specific claim that relative is more general is also false. This is a dialectical strategy of refuting the more general to invalidate the specific.

B) Al-naw' al-haqiqi is absolutely more general than al-naw' al-idafi.

D) They are related by 'umum min wajh (generality from one aspect).

Key Takeaway
The relationship between real and relative species is generality from one aspect ('umum min wajh), not absolute generality; understanding this refutes the false claim that relative species is always more general.

Refuting the Absolute Generality Claim
Refuting absolute generality claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of counterexamples used to refute the claim that accidental species is absolutely more general than true species.

Core Idea
Accidental and true species are not in absolute generality; each can exist without the other.

The ancient logicians, including Ibn Sina, held that accidental species is absolutely more general than true species. However, our author refutes this by showing that each can exist without the other. In this lesson, we will examine the refutation and learn how to identify such counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Locate the statement: 'accidental species is absolutely more general than true species'. Understand that this is the position of Ibn Sina and the ancients.
2
Find the counterexample for accidental species without true species
The author gives intermediate species (e.g., animal) as accidental species that are not true species because they are genera. Recognize that intermediate species are not ultimate species.
Intermediate species are species relative to something above but genera to something below.
3
Find the counterexample for true species without accidental species
The author gives simple realities (e.g., intellect, soul) as true species that are not accidental species because they are not genera. Understand that if they were accidental, they would be composite.
Simple realities have no genus, so they cannot be accidental species.
4
Understand why these examples disprove absolute generality
Since each type can exist without the other, the relationship cannot be one of absolute generality in either direction. This sets up the need for a different relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الكلي والجزئي وقد ذهب قدماء المنطقيين حتى الشيخ في كتاب الشفاء إلى أن النوع الإضافي أعم مطلقاً من الحقيقي، وردّ ذلك في صورة دعوى أعم، وهي أن ليس بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلقاً، فإن كلا منهما موجود بدون الآخر. أما وجود النوع الإضافي بدون الحقيقي، فكما في الأنواع المتوسطة فإنها أنواع إضافية، وليست أنواعاً حقيقية لأنها أجناس. وأما وجود النوع الحقيقي بدون الإضافي فكما في الحقائق البسيطة - كالعقل والنفس والنقطة والوحدة - فإنها أنواع حقيقية وليست أنواعاً إضافية، وإلا لكانت مركبة لوجود اندراج النوع الإضافي تحت جنس، فيكون مركباً من الجنس والفصل. ثم بين ما هو الحق عنده، وهو أن بينهما عموماً وخصوصاً من وجه، لأنه قد ثبت وجود كل منهما بدون الآخر، وهما متصادقان على النوع السافل لأنه نوع حقيقي من حيث أنه مقول على أفراد متفقة الحقيقة، ونوع إضافي من حيث أنه مقول عليه وعلى غيره الجنس في جواب ماهو.
Topics of the universal and the particular. The ancient logicians, including the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) in his book al-Shifa', held that the accidental species is absolutely more general than the true species. This was refuted in the form of a more general claim, namely that there is no absolute generality and specificity between them, for each exists without the other. As for the existence of the accidental species without the true species, it is like the intermediate species, for they are accidental species and are not true species because they are genera. As for the existence of the true species without the accidental species, it is like the simple realities – such as the intellect, the soul, the point, and the unit – for they are true species and are not accidental species; otherwise they would be compounded due to the inclusion of the accidental species under a genus, thus being composed of genus and differentia. Then he explained what is correct in his view, which is that between them there is generality and specificity from one aspect, because it has been established that each exists without the other, and they both apply to the lowest species: it is a true species in that it is predicated of individuals agreeing in reality, and an accidental species in that it is predicated of it and of something else as a genus in answer to 'what is it?'.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses the relationship between two types of species: the accidental species (al-naw' al-idafi) and the true species (al-naw' al-haqiqi). The author begins by stating the position of the ancient logicians, including Ibn Sina, that the accidental species is absolutely more general than the true species. He then refutes this by presenting a stronger claim: that there is no absolute generality at all between them, because each can exist without the other. He gives examples: intermediate species (like 'animal' for 'horse') are accidental species but not true species, because they are genera relative to lower species. Simple realities (like 'intellect' or 'point') are true species but not accidental species, because they are not genera; if they were accidental species, they would be composite. Finally, the author presents the correct view: there is partial overlap (umum wa khusus min wajh). They coincide in the lowest species (al-naw' al-safil), which is a true species when considered as predicated of individuals with the same reality, and an accidental species when considered as a genus for something else. The hashiya notes (from Sharif al-Jurjani) provide further clarification, e.g., that the simple realities must be proper quiddities not falling under a genus, and that there are possible objections to the examples.

Key Terms
النوع الإضافي
النوع الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Accidental species and true species are not in a relation of absolute generality; each can exist without the other.

Establishing Partial Overlap
Establishing partial overlap

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the partial overlap (umum wa khusus min wajh) between accidental species and true species, and identify the lowest species as the point of intersection.

Core Idea
The lowest species is both a true species and an accidental species, showing that the two types partially overlap.

After refuting the claim of absolute generality, the author presents the correct relationship: partial generality and specificity (umum wa khusus min wajh). This relationship is illustrated by the lowest species (al-naw' al-safil), which is both a true species and an accidental species. In this lesson, we will understand how this partial overlap works and why it is the correct view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the correct relationship stated by the author
The author says: 'between them there is generality and specificity from one aspect' (baina-huma umum wa khusus min wajh). Understand that this is partial overlap.
2
Recognize the justification: each exists without the other
The author reiterates that each type can exist without the other, which has already been established. This eliminates the possibility of absolute generality in either direction.
3
Find the point of overlap: the lowest species
The lowest species (e.g., 'human' for 'animal' and 'human') is both a true species (predicated of individuals agreeing in reality) and an accidental species (predicated of itself and something else as a genus in answer to 'what is it?').
The lowest species is the most specific species under which only individuals fall, but it can also be considered an accidental species relative to a higher genus.
4
Understand why the lowest species is both
As a true species, it is predicated of individuals sharing the same essence. As an accidental species, it serves as a genus relative to its individuals when answering 'what is it?' (i.e., the species is a broader category than the individual).
This dual aspect is what makes it the point of overlap.
5
Summarize the partial overlap relationship
Accidental species are more general in some aspects (they include intermediate species), true species are more general in others (they include simple realities), and they coincide in the lowest species.

Differentiae: Constitutive and Divisive Roles
Rules for constitutive and divisive differentiae across universal hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given universal requires a constitutive differentia, a divisive differentia, both, or neither, based on its position in the hierarchy.

Core Idea
A differentia either constitutes a species or divides a genus—or both.

In this passage, the author explains how differentiae function differently depending on whether the universal is a highest genus, a lowest species, or an intermediate. Understanding these rules is essential for constructing correct definitions in any Islamic science, from usul al-fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal's level
Determine whether the universal is the highest genus (e.g., jawhar), the lowest species (e.g., insān), or an intermediate (e.g., hayawān).
The level dictates what differentiae are possible or required.
2
Apply rule for highest genus
A highest genus may have a constitutive differentia (to make it a species of a higher genus, though it is highest) and must have a divisive differentia to split it into species.
For jawhar, a divisive differentia like 'rational' yields animal? Actually jawhar is divided by 'body' etc.
3
Apply rule for lowest species
A lowest species must have a constitutive differentia (to complete its essence) and cannot have a divisive differentia because it has no species below it.
For insān, the constitutive differentia is 'rational'; it cannot be divided into further species.
4
Apply rule for intermediate genera
An intermediate genus must have both: a constitutive differentia to define it as a species of a higher genus, and a divisive differentia to split it into lower species.
For hayawān, 'sensitive' constitutes it as a species of 'living body', while 'rational' divides it into man and non-rational animals.
5
Apply the transfer rules
A differentia that constitutes a higher genus also constitutes all species under it. Conversely, a differentia that divides a lower species also divides all its higher genera.
Example: 'Sensitive' constitutes hayawān, so it also constitutes insān. 'Rational' divides insān from other animals, so it also divides hayawān.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والجنس العالي جاز أن يكون له فصل يقومه، لجواز تركبـه مـن أمرين متساويين أو أمور متساوية ويجب أن يكون له فصل يقسمه. والنوع السافل يجب أن يكون له فصل يقومه ويمتنع أن يكون له فصل يقسمه.
والمتوسطات يجب أن يكون لها فصول تقسمها وفصول تقومهـا وكـلّ فصل يقوم العالي فهو يقوم السافل من غير عكس كلي، وكل فصل يقسم السافل فهو يقسم العالي من غير عكس.
القول : الفصل له نسبة إلى النوع ونسبة إلى الجنس – أي جنس

G
فإن الواقع أنسب بالمدلول مطابقة، والداخل أنسب بالمدلول تضمناً، وإن كان لكل منهما مناسبة مع كل من الجزأين (شريف).
He said: The highest genus is allowed to have a differentia that constitutes it, because it is possible for it to be composed of two co-equal things or co-equal matters, and it must have a differentia that divides it. The lowest species must have a differentia that constitutes it, and it is impossible for it to have a differentia that divides it. The intermediate genera must have differentiae that divide them and differentiae that constitute them. And every differentia that constitutes the higher constitutes the lower, not universally conversely; and every differentia that divides the lower divides the higher, not universally conversely. The statement: The differentia has a relation to the species and a relation to the genus — i.e., the genus of that species. For 'occuring' (wāqi‘) is more appropriate for the meaning by congruence, and 'entering' (dākhil) is more appropriate for the meaning by inclusion, although each has appropriateness with each of the two parts (Sharif).
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage lays out the rules governing how differentiae (فصول) relate to genera and species. The author distinguishes three cases: the highest genus, the lowest species, and intermediate genera. For the highest genus, it is possible to have a differentia that constitutes it (e.g., a differentia that gives it existence as a species under a higher genus? Actually the highest genus itself cannot be constituted by a differentia from its own perspective; the text says 'jāza an yakūna lahu faslun yuqawwimuhu' meaning it is possible that the highest genus has a differentia that constitutes it – this likely refers to the fact that a genus can be constituted by a differentia when considered as a species of a higher genus? The interpretation: the highest genus (like substance) can be considered as a genus relative to something else, but it itself may be composed of co-equal constituents? The text also says it must have a differentia that divides it, meaning that a genus must be divisible into species by differentiae. For the lowest species, it must have a differentia that constitutes it (forming its essence), but it cannot have a differentia that divides it because it has no species below it. For intermediate genera (like 'animal'), they must have both: differentiae that divide them into species and differentiae that constitute them as species of a higher genus. The passage then gives a key rule: if a differentia constitutes the higher genus, it also constitutes the lower species that falls under it (e.g. 'rational' constitutes 'animal'? Actually 'rational' constitutes 'man' among animals; but as a differentia of 'animal'? Need careful: the rule is: every differentia that constitutes the higher constitutes the lower, but not conversely. Example: If 'sensitive' constitutes 'animal' (as a species of 'living body'), then it also constitutes 'man'? Actually 'sensitive' is a differentia of 'animal' but not of 'man'? The commentary must clarify. The text likely means: if a differentia constitutes a genus (i.e., makes it a species under a higher genus), then that same differentia also constitutes the species under that genus (i.e., all species share that differential characteristic). Conversely, a differentia that divides the lower (i.e., distinguishes species under a genus) also divides the higher genus. For example, 'rational' divides 'animal' (since some animals are rational, some not) and also divides the higher genus 'living body'. The last part is a commentary by the editor (Sharif) relating the terms 'occurring' and 'entering' to the two kinds of parts from the previous lesson.

The role of a differentia—constitutive or divisive—depends on the universal's level in the hierarchy: highest genera require division, lowest species require constitution, and intermediates require both.

The High Genus: Constitutive and Divisive Differentiae
Author explains the high genus may have a constitutive differentia but must have a divisive one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given genus is high and determine if it can have a constitutive differentia and must have a divisive differentia.

Core Idea
The high genus may have a constitutive differentia but must have a divisive one.

In this passage, the author explains the logical constraints on the highest genus. The high genus, being the most universal category without any genus above it, has a special status regarding differentiae. Understanding this helps us see the structure of the Porphyrian tree and how definitions cascade downward. We will explore the possibility of a constitutive differentia and the necessity of a divisive differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the high genus
A high genus is one that has no genus above it. It is the most universal category in a given hierarchy.
2
Understand constitutive differentia
A constitutive differentia combines with a genus to form a species. For the high genus, if it had one, it would be composed of two co-extensive components that together equal it.
The ancients denied this because they argued every quiddity with a constitutive differentia must have a genus, which the high genus lacks.
3
Appreciate the ancients' objection
The ancients held that if something has a constitutive differentia, then that differentia and the genus together form a composite, implying there is a higher genus. Since the high genus has no genus above, it cannot have a constitutive differentia.
4
Grasp the necessity of divisive differentia
The high genus must have species under it. To distinguish those species, divisive differentiae are needed. Thus, the high genus must have at least one divisive differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا تصورت هذا فنقول : الجنس العالي جاز أن يكون له فصل يقومه، لجواز أن يتركب من أمرين متساويين يساويانه ويميّزانه عن مشاركاته في الوجود، وقد امتنع القدماء عن ذلك بناء على أن كل ماهيّة لها فصل يقومها لابد أن يكون لها جنس - وقد سلف ذلك - (۲ - ويجب أن يكون له - أي للجنس العالي - فصل يقسمه، لوجوب أن يكون تحته أنواع وفصول الأنواع بالقياس إلى الجنس مقسمات له.
When you have understood this, we say: The high genus may have a differentia that constitutes it, because it is possible that it is composed of two co-extensive things that equal it and distinguish it from its co-participants in existence, and the ancients prevented this based on the principle that every quiddity that has a constitutive differentia must have a genus—and this has preceded. And it must have—i.e., the high genus—a differentia that divides it, because it is necessary that beneath it there are species, and the differentiae of the species in relation to the genus are divisive of it.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage discusses the high genus (الجنس العالي), i.e., the genus that has no genus above it. The author states two points: (1) It is possible (jā'iz) for the high genus to have a constitutive differentia (فصل يقومه), because it could be composed of two co-extensive (mutasāwiyān) components that together equal it and distinguish it from other things. However, the ancients (al-qudamā') forbade this, arguing that any quiddity with a constitutive differentia must have a genus (which the high genus by definition lacks). (2) It is necessary (wājib) for the high genus to have a divisive differentia (فصل يقسمه), because it must have species under it, and the differentiae of those species serve to divide the genus. This clarifies the logical constraints on the highest universal in the Porphyrian tree.

Key Takeaway
The high genus, though it can conceptually be composed of co-extensive parts, always needs a divisive differentia to generate its species.

Low species and intermediates: constitutive and divisive differentiae
Low species must have constitutive differentia; intermediates have both; relation of high and low differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given universal can have a constitutive and/or divisive differentia based on its position in the hierarchy and apply the principle that higher differentiae necessarily constitute lower ones.

Core Idea
Low species have only constitutive differentiae; intermediates have both; higher differentiae entail lower ones but not vice versa.

This passage clarifies the logical requirements for differentiae in the Porphyrian tree. Understanding which differentiae a universal can possess is essential for defining terms correctly in Islamic sciences. The text also introduces a key principle about the transitivity of constitutiveness, which prevents collapsing the hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims about the low species
The low species must have a constitutive differentia (because it has a genus above it that needs distinguishing) and cannot have a divisive differentia (because it has no species below it).
Think of 'human' as a low species under 'animal'.
2
Understand the rationale for intermediates
Intermediates (e.g., 'animal' or 'body') are both species relative to a higher genus and genera relative to lower species. Hence they require both constitutive differentiae (to be distinguished from co-species) and divisive differentiae (to divide into their species).
3
Grasp the transitivity principle
Every differentia that constitutes a higher universal also constitutes the lower, because the higher is constitutive of the lower. The principle 'muqawwim al-muqawwim muqawwim' is invoked: if A constitutes B and B constitutes C, then A constitutes C.
This is directional: all constitutives of the high are in the low, but not all of the low are in the high.
4
Recognize the asymmetry
The converse is not true: not every constitutive differentia of the low is constitutive of the high. If it were, there would be no difference between low and high. The low may have additional properties beyond the shared constitutive differentiae.
This preserves the distinction between levels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع السافل يجب أن يكون له فصل مقوم، ويمتنع أن يكون له فصل مقسم : أما الأول : فلوجوب أن يكون فوقه جنس، وما له جنس لابد أن يكون له فصل يميزه عن مشاركاته في ذلك الجنس. وأما الثاني : فلامتناع أن يكون تحته أنواع، وإلا لم يكن سافلاً - بل متوسطاً - والمتوسطات - سواء كانت أنواعاً أو أجناساً - يجب أن يكون لها فصول مقومات - لأن فوقها أجناساً - وفصول مقسمات - لأن تحتها أنواعاً - فكل فصل يقوم النوع العالي أو الجنس العالي فهو يقوم السافل، لأنّ العالي مقوم السافل، ومقوم المقوم مقوم عن غير عكس كلّي - أي ليس كل مقوم للسافل فهو مقوم للعالي، لأنه قد ثبت أن جميع مقومات العالي مقومات للسافل ، فلو كان جميع مقومات السافل مقومات العالي لم يكن بين السافل والعالي فرق. (۱) لم يذكر النوع العالي لاندراجه في الجنس المتوسط ولا الجنس السافل لاندراجه في النوع المتوسط (شريف). (٢) أراد بالعالي هاهنا الفوقاني، وبالسافل التحتاني، لاما مر من أن العالي ما هو فوق الجميع، والسافل ما هو تحت الجميع (شريف). (۳) وذلك لأن العالي لما كان مقوماً للسافل كان جميع مقوماته – فصولاً كانت أو أجناساً - مقومات للسافل قطعاً (شريف). (٤) أي جميع الفصول المقومة له، لأن الكلام فيها. فإن قلت: فعلى هذا لا يلزم عدم الفرق بين السافل والعالي، لجواز أن يكون في السافل سوى الفصول المقومة المشتركة بينه وبين العالي فرضاً - أمر آخر يمتاز به عن العالي.
The low species must have a constitutive differentia, and it is impossible for it to have a divisive differentia. As for the first: because it must have a genus above it, and whatever has a genus must have a differentia distinguishing it from its co-participants in that genus. As for the second: because it is impossible that there are species beneath it, otherwise it would not be low—rather it would be intermediate. And the intermediates—whether they are species or genera—must have constitutive differentiae (because above them are genera) and divisive differentiae (because beneath them are species). So every differentia that constitutes the high species or the high genus constitutes the low [species or genus], because the high is constitutive of the low, and the constitutive of the constitutive is constitutive, without full converse—i.e., not every constitutive of the low is constitutive of the high, because it has been established that all constitutives of the high are constitutives of the low. For if all constitutives of the low were constitutives of the high, there would be no difference between the low and the high. (1) He did not mention the high species because it is included in the intermediate genus, nor the low genus because it is included in the intermediate species (Sharif). (2) By 'high' here he means the higher, and by 'low' the lower, not the absolute highest and lowest (Sharif). (3) That is because when the high is constitutive of the low, all its constitutives—whether differentiae or genera—are certainly constitutives of the low (Sharif). (4) i.e., all the constitutive differentiae of it, because the discussion is about them. If you say: thus no difference between low and high is necessitated, for it is possible that in the low, besides the constitutive differentiae shared with the high, there is another thing by which it is distinguished from the high (Sharif).
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage discusses the requirements for differentiae in the low species (النوع السافل) and intermediates (المتوسطات). The low species must have a constitutive differentia (فصل مقوم) because it has a genus above it, and any genus requires a differentia to distinguish its species. It cannot have a divisive differentia (فصل مقسم) because that would require species beneath it, making it intermediate. Intermediates—whether they are species (like 'animal') or genera (like 'body')—must have both: constitutive differentiae because they have a genus above, and divisive differentiae because they have species below. The passage then states an important principle: every differentia that constitutes a higher entity (high species or high genus) also constitutes the lower entity, because the higher is constitutive of the lower. However, the converse is not true: not every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher. This is because all constitutives of the higher are necessarily constitutives of the lower, but if all constitutives of the lower were also of the higher, there would be no difference between them. The footnotes by Sharif clarify that 'high' and 'low' here are relative, and that the high species is included in the intermediate genus. A potential objection is raised: if the low shares all constitutive differentiae with the high, how do they differ? The answer is that the low may have an additional component besides the shared differentiae.

فصل مقوم
فصل مقسم
المتوسطات

Key Takeaway
A universal's position in the hierarchy determines its permissible differentiae: low species only constitutive, intermediates both, and higher differentiae necessarily propagate downward but not upward.

The Lowest Species: Constitutive and Divisive Differentiae in Hierarchical Definitions
Explains why the lowest species has a constitutive differentia but not a divisive one, and the hierarchical relationship of differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between constitutive and divisive differentiae, explain why the lowest species has one and not the other, and trace the hierarchical relationship using the example of man (insan).

Core Idea
The lowest species needs one differentia to stand out; it can't divide because it has no species below.

In Islamic logic, understanding differentiae (fusul) is crucial for proper definition. This lesson examines a key rule: the lowest species must have a constitutive differentia to be distinguished from other species under the same genus, yet it cannot have a divisive differentia because it has no subspecies. Moreover, we explore how differentiae operate across hierarchical levels, using the concrete example of man's gradual definition from substance to animal.

Guided Path
1
Define the lowest species
Al-naw' al-safil is the species that has no species below it in the hierarchy. It is the terminal point of division.
In the hierarchy of genera and species, it is the most specific category.
2
Understand the two claims
The author states: (a) the lowest species must have a constitutive differentia; (b) it cannot have a divisive differentia. Grasp both as complementary rules.
3
Reason for the first claim
Because the lowest species has a genus above it. Anything with a genus needs a differentia to distinguish it from other species sharing that genus. Without it, it would not be a distinct species.
Example: man under animal is distinguished by 'rational'.
4
Reason for the second claim
If the lowest species had a divisive differentia, it would divide it into species below, contradicting its status as lowest. Only intermediate species/genera have both constitutive and divisive differentiae.
5
Explore reciprocity in hierarchy
Every differentia that constitutes the higher also constitutes the lower (since the higher is a constituent of the lower), but not vice versa. Similarly, every differentia dividing the lower divides the higher, but not vice versa.
The author emphasizes partial reciprocity to avoid conflating levels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع السافل يجب أن يكون له فصل مقوم، ويمتنع أن يكون له فصل مقسم : أما الأول : فلوجوب أن يكون فوقه جنس، وما له جنس لابد أن يكون له فصل يميزه عن مشاركاته في ذلك الجنس. وأما الثاني : فلامتناع أن يكون تحته أنواع، وإلا لم يكن سافلاً - بل متوسطاً - والمتوسطات - سواء كانت أنواعاً أو أجناساً" - يجب أن يكون لها فصول مقومات - لأن فوقها أجناساً - وفصول مقسمات - لأن تحتها أنواعاً - فكل فصل يقوم النوع العالي أو الجنس العالي فهو يقوم السافل، لأنّ العالي مقوم السافل، ومقوم المقوم مقوم عن غير عكس كلّي - أي ليس كل مقوم للسافل فهو مقوم للعالي، لأنه قد ثبت أن جميع مقومات العالي مقومات للسافل ، فلو كان جميع مقومات السافل مقومات العالي لم يكن بين السافل والعالي فرق. (۱) لم يذكر النوع العالي لاندراجه في الجنس المتوسط ولا الجنس السافل لاندراجه في النوع المتوسط (شريف). (٢) أراد بالعالي هاهنا الفوقاني، وبالسافل التحتاني، لاما مر من أن العالي ما هو فوق الجميع، والسافل ما هو تحت الجميع (شريف). (۳) وذلك لأن العالي لما كان مقوماً للسافل كان جميع مقوماته – فصولاً كانت أو أجناساً - مقومات للسافل قطعاً (شريف). (٤) أي جميع الفصول المقومة له، لأن الكلام فيها. فإن قلت: فعلى هذا لا يلزم عدم الفرق بين السافل والعالي، لجواز أن يكون في السافل سوى الفصول المقومة المشتركة بينه وبين العالي فرضاً - أمر آخر يمتاز به عن العالي. وإنما قال : من غير عكس كلّي لأن بعض مقوم السافل مقوّم للعالي فهو مقوم للعالي. وكل فصل يقسم الجنس السافل فهو يقسم العالي، لأن معنى تقسيم السافل تحصيله في نوع، وكل ما يحصل السافل في نوع يحصل العالي فيه، فيكون العالي حاصلاً أيضاً في ذلك النوع ؛ وهو معنى تقسيمه للعالي. ولا ينعكس كلياً، أي ليس كل مقسم العالي مقسم السافل، لأنّ فصل السافل مقسم للعالي وهو لا يقسم السافل بل يقومه، ولكنه ينعكس جزئياً، فإن بعض مقسم العالي مقسم للسائل، وهو مقسم السافل. قلت: ليس في السافل وراء ماهيّة العالي إلا الفصول المقومة للمسافل، فإن فرضت مشتركة اتحد السافل والعالي ماهيّة، مثلاً ليس في الإنسان وراء الجوهر إلا فصول مقومة للإنسان ومقسّمة للجوهر، و هي قابل الأبعاد الثلاثة والنامي والحسّاس والمتحرك بالإرادة والناطق ؛ وكذا ليس في الإنسان وراء الجسم إلا فصول مقومة للإنسان ومقسمة للجسم هي الثلاثة الأخيرة، وليس فيه أيضاً وراء الجسم النامي إلا فصلان مقومان له ومقسمان للجسم النامي - هما الأخيران - وليس فيه أيضاً وراء الحيوان إلا فصل واحد هو الناطق، فإنّه إذا ترتبت الأجناس كان الذي تحت الجنس العالي مركباً منه ومن فصل - وهكذا - فلايتميز السافل عن الذي فوقه إلا بما هو فصل مقوّم له، فإذا فرض كونه مشتركاً لم يبق بينهما فرق أصلاً (شريف).
The lowest species must have a constitutive differentia, and it is impossible for it to have a divisive differentia. As for the first: because it must have a genus above it, and whatever has a genus must have a differentia that distinguishes it from its participants in that genus. As for the second: because it is impossible for it to have species below it; otherwise it would not be lowest but intermediate. The intermediates—whether species or genera—must have constitutive differentiae because they have genera above them, and divisive differentiae because they have species below them. So every differentia that constitutes the higher species or the higher genus constitutes the lower, because the higher is constitutive of the lower, and the constitutive of the constitutive is constitutive, but without total reciprocity—i.e., not every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher, because it is established that all constitutives of the higher are constitutives of the lower; if all constitutives of the lower were constitutives of the higher, there would be no difference between the lower and the higher. (1) He did not mention the higher species because it is included in the intermediate genus, nor the lower genus because it is included in the intermediate species (Sharif). (2) He means by 'higher' here the superior, and by 'lower' the inferior, not what was previously said that the higher is what is above all and the lower is what is below all (Sharif). (3) That is because when the higher is constitutive of the lower, all its constitutives—whether differentiae or genera—are necessarily constitutives of the lower (Sharif). (4) That is, all the constitutive differentiae of it, because the discussion is about them. If you say: then on this basis, there is no necessary lack of difference between the lower and the higher, because it is possible that in the lower, besides the constitutive differentiae assumed common between it and the higher, there is another thing by which it is distinguished from the higher. And he only said 'without total reciprocity' because some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher, so it is constitutive of the higher. And every differentia that divides the lower genus divides the higher, because the meaning of dividing the lower is to realize it in a species, and whatever realizes the lower in a species realizes the higher in it, so the higher is also realized in that species; that is the meaning of its dividing the higher. And it is not totally reciprocal, i.e., not every divider of the higher is a divider of the lower, because the differentia of the lower divides the higher and it does not divide the lower but constitutes it; but it is partially reciprocal, for some dividers of the higher are dividers of the lower, which are the dividers of the lower. I say: There is nothing in the lower beyond the quiddity of the higher except the constitutive differentiae of the lowers; if they are assumed common, then the lower and the higher would be one in quiddity. For example, there is nothing in man beyond the substance (jawhar) except differentiae that constitute man and divide substance, namely: receptive of three dimensions, growing, sentient, moving by will, and rational. Likewise, there is nothing in man beyond body (jism) except differentiae that constitute man and divide body—these are the last three. And there is nothing in him beyond the growing body (jism nabat) except two differentiae that constitute him and divide the growing body—these are the last two. And there is nothing in him beyond animal (hayawan) except one differentia, which is the rational. For when the genera are arranged, that which is under the higher genus is composed of it and a differentia—and so on—so the lower is not distinguished from what is above it except by what is a constitutive differentia for it. If it is assumed common, there remains absolutely no difference between them (Sharif).
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage discusses the properties of the lowest species (al-naw' al-safil) with respect to constitutive (muqawwim) and divisive (muqassim) differentiae. The author begins by stating two key points: (1) The lowest species must have a constitutive differentia, and (2) it is impossible for it to have a divisive differentia. The reason for the first is that it must have a genus above it, and anything that has a genus needs a differentia to distinguish it from other species sharing that genus. For example, man (insan) as a species under animal (hayawan) needs the differentia 'rational' (natiq) to distinguish it from other animals. The reason for the second is that if the lowest species had a divisive differentia, it would divide it into subspecies, contradicting its being the lowest (safil). Only intermediate genera/species have both constitutive and divisive differentiae. The author then explains the relationship between higher and lower genera/species: any differentia that constitutes the higher also constitutes the lower, because the higher is a constituent of the lower. However, this is not totally reciprocal—not every constitutive differentia of the lower is constitutive of the higher. The commentary (from al-Sharif) clarifies that the term 'higher' here means the immediate higher genus, not the highest genus. The author also addresses the claim about divisive differentiae: any differentia that divides the lower genus also divides the higher, because dividing the lower means realizing it under a species, and that realization inevitably includes the higher. This is also not totally reciprocal, because the differentia of the lower divides the higher but does not divide the lower; rather it constitutes the lower. Finally, the author provides a concrete example: the species 'man' (insan) relative to its successive higher genera: substance (jawhar), body (jism), growing body (jism nabat), and animal (hayawan). Each additional differentia beyond the higher genus constitutes man and divides that higher genus. Thus, the lowest species is distinguished from its higher genus only by its constitutive differentia; if that differentia were shared, there would be no difference at all.

Key Terms

الفصل المقوم
الفصل المقسم

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the lowest species have a constitutive differentia?
A) Because it has species below it that need to be differentiated.
B) Because it has a genus above it, and anything with a genus needs a differentia to distinguish it from other species in that genus.
C) Because constitutive differentiae are necessary for any species.
D) Because without it, the species would be a genus.

Key Takeaway
The lowest species is defined by a constitutive differentia that sets it apart from its immediate higher genus, and it cannot be further divided by a divisive differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a hierarchical chain of genera for the species 'hajj' (pilgrimage) using the model of man: start with 'act of worship' as the highest genus and add successive differentiae until you reach the lowest species 'hajj'. Then identify the constitutive differentia of hajj and explain why it cannot have a divisive differentia.
?
Consider the definition of 'riba' (usury) in Fiqh. If riba is defined as 'an exchange with an excess', what are its genus and differentia? Is this a complete definition? Discuss using the principles from this lesson.

Module 6 · Lesson 95

The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Differentia: Dividing Higher Genus, Constituting Lower
Author explains how a differentia divides the higher genus while constituting the lower genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the relationship between a differentia and higher/lower genera, identifying which differentiae constitute and which divide.

Core Idea
A differentia simultaneously constitutes the lower genus and divides the higher genus.

In Islamic logic, definitions rely on genus and differentia. This passage from al-Qutbi's sharh clarifies a subtle but crucial point: a differentia that constitutes a lower genus (like 'rational' for 'human') also divides the higher genus (like 'animal' or 'substance'). But not every differentia that divides a higher genus necessarily divides a lower one. Understanding this asymmetry is essential for constructing valid definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about total conversion
The author states that a differentia dividing the lower genus also divides the higher, but the converse is not universally true. Find the phrase 'without total conversion' and understand why it is mentioned.
The phrase 'من غير عكس كلّي' indicates the asymmetry.
2
Understand the reason for the rule
The author explains that dividing the lower genus means specifying it into a species, which automatically places the higher genus in that same species. So any differentia that does this for the lower also does it for the higher.
This is why every differentia that divides the lower also divides the higher.
3
Recognize the partial conversion
While total conversion fails, partial conversion holds: some dividers of the higher genus also divide the lower, specifically the differentia of the lower genus itself.
The differentia of the lower genus divides the higher but does not divide the lower (it constitutes it).
4
Examine the hierarchy example
Study the example of human (insān) under substance (jawhar), body (jism), growing body (jism nāmī), and animal (ḥayawān). List the differentiae at each level and see how they both constitute human and divide the higher genus.
The example shows that lower levels have fewer differentiae, each differentiating from the higher.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قال : من غير عكس كلّي لأن بعض مقوم السافل مقوّم للعالي فهو مقوم للعالي.
وكل فصل يقسم الجنس السافل فهو يقسم العالي، لأن معنى تقسيم السافل تحصيله في نوع، وكل ما يحصل السافل في نوع يحصل العالي فيه، فيكون العالي حاصلاً أيضاً في ذلك النوع ؛ وهو معنى تقسيمه للعالي. ولا ينعكس كلياً، أي ليس كل مقسم العالي مقسم السافل، لأنّ فصل السافل مقسم للعالي وهو لا يقسم السافل بل يقومه، ولكنه ينعكس جزئياً، فإن بعض مقسم العالي مقسم للسائل، وهو مقسم السافل.
قلت: ليس في السافل وراء ماهيّة العالي إلا الفصول المقومة للمسافل، فإن فرضت مشتركة اتحد السافل والعالي ماهيّة، مثلاً ليس في الإنسان وراء الجوهر إلا فصول مقومة للإنسان ومقسّمة للجوهر، و هي قابل الأبعاد الثلاثة والنامي والحسّاس والمتحرك بالإرادة والناطق ؛ وكذا ليس في الإنسان وراء الجسم إلا فصول مقومة للإنسان ومقسمة للجسم هي الثلاثة الأخيرة، وليس فيه أيضاً وراء الجسم النامي إلا فصلان مقومان له ومقسمان للجسم النامي - هما الأخيران - وليس فيه أيضاً وراء الحيوان إلا فصل واحد هو الناطق، فإنّه إذا ترتبت الأجناس كان الذي تحت الجنس العالي مركباً منه ومن فصل - وهكذا - فلايتميز السافل عن الذي فوقه إلا بما هو فصل مقوّم له، فإذا فرض كونه مشتركاً لم يبق بينهما فرق أصلاً (شريف).
He only said 'without total conversion' because some of what constitutes the lower constitutes the higher, so it constitutes the higher. And every differentia that divides the lower genus divides the higher, because the meaning of dividing the lower is realizing it in a species, and everything that realizes the lower in a species realizes the higher in it, so the higher is also realized in that species; that is the meaning of its dividing the higher. And it does not convert totally, i.e., not every divider of the higher is a divider of the lower, because the differentia of the lower is a divider of the higher while it does not divide the lower but constitutes it, yet it converts partially, for some dividers of the higher are dividers of the lower, namely the differentia of the lower. I say: In the lower, beyond the essence of the higher, there are only the differentiae that constitute the lower genera. If they are assumed to be shared, the lower and the higher would be identical in essence. For example, in human (insān), beyond substance (jawhar), there are only differentiae that constitute human and divide substance: these are 'capable of the three dimensions', 'growing', 'sensing', 'voluntarily moving', and 'rational'. Similarly, in human beyond body (jism) there are only differentiae that constitute human and divide body: the last three. And also in it beyond the growing body (jism nāmī) there are only two differentiae that constitute it and divide the growing body: the last two. And also in it beyond animal (ḥayawān) there is only one differentia: 'rational'. For when the genera are arranged, that which is under the higher genus is composed of it and a differentia—and so on—so the lower is not distinguished from that above it except by what is a differentia constituting it. If it is assumed to be shared, no difference remains between them at all (Sharīf).
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharḥ) of a previous matn statement about the relationship between differentiae and genera. The author first clarifies the phrase 'without total conversion': some constitutives of the lower genus are also constitutives of the higher genus, so they constitute the higher. He then states the rule: every differentia that divides the lower genus also divides the higher genus. The reason is that dividing the lower genus means specifying it into a species, and the same specification places the higher genus in that species as well. However, the converse is not universally true: not every divider of the higher genus divides the lower genus. For example, the differentia of the lower genus divides the higher but does not divide the lower; instead, it constitutes the lower. Partial conversion does hold: some dividers of the higher are dividers of the lower, namely the differentia of the lower itself. The author then provides an extensive example from the genus of substance down to human. In human, beyond substance, there are five differentiae: 'capable of the three dimensions' (i.e., corporeality), 'growing', 'sensing', 'voluntarily moving', and 'rational'. These both constitute human and divide substance. Moving down the hierarchy, human beyond body (jism) has only the last three differentiae, and so on. This illustrates that the lower genus is only distinguished from the higher by its constitutive differentiae. If those differentiae were shared, the two would be identical in essence, which is impossible.

مقوم
مقسم
جنس
سافل
عالي
عكس كلي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the differentiae of the lower genus were completely shared with the higher genus, what would be the result?
A) The lower genus would be more specific.
B) The lower genus would be the same in essence as the higher genus.
C) The differentia would no longer be constitutive.
D) The lower genus would become a differentia itself.

Key Takeaway
A differentia that constitutes a lower genus also divides the higher genus, but not every divider of the higher divides the lower; only the differentia of the lower genus does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the hierarchy: substance → body → growing body → animal → human, list all differentiae that constitute human at each level and explain which higher genera each differentia divides.
?
Construct a similar hierarchy for another species, e.g., horse, and identify its constitutive and divisive differentiae, referencing the pattern in the passage.
